f% ANTON, Ohio, Sept. 18. â Throughout the afternoon the human tide pressed steadily forward to
+J the bier. Four abreast in double line, without a moment's halt the people hurried by the casket and
then, with tear-stained faces, passed out of the darkened chamber into the bright sunlight. In the line
were those representing all stations in life, all ages, all conditions. At one moment an pld man, bent
and witJiered, who had known "Mother" McKinley, tottered up to gaze upon the silent features. He
was followed by a man of middle age, who had gone to school with McKinley. Over on one side was
the Knight Templar who had marched beside McKinley in parades. All who came had some recol
lection of personal relation with the man lying there cold in death. Fathers brought their children and
held them over the bier for a fleeting glance at the upturned face. 'A sleeping baby zvas aroused by its*
mother for a sight its little mind could not comprehend. Toilers came from the factories qnd dinner
pails in hand, trooped by the bier of him whom they called their benefactor and their friend. A great
flood of emotion was welling up in the Jiearts of these sojrowing people. y

SAN FRANCISCO, THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER \19, 1901.
The San Francisco Call.At the meeting of the Cabinet yesterday
President Roosevelt amplified the an
nouncement which he made on taking the
oath by declaring specifically that the pol
icies which were outlined in Mr. McKin
ley's Buffalo speech were to be carried
out. In the language of the speech these
are. "Policies of good will and friendly
trade relations."
Policies Number Four.
They are four in number. Foremost Mr.
McKinley placed the policy of entering
Into reciprocity treaties with other na
tions and making concessions from the
high tariff rates of the Dingley law on
goods imported from foreign countries In
return for reciprocal concessions on goods
imported into these countries from the
Vnited States.
"If perchance," said he, "some of our
tariffs are no longer needed for revenue
or to encourage or protect our industries
at home, Â¦why should they not be em
ployed to extend and promote our mar
ked abroad?"
The eecond policy for which Mr. Mc-
Kinley declared in this last speech of his
life was for the development of the mer
chant marine of the United States â for
more ships under the American flag, built
and manned and owned by Americans.
The third policy referred to in the Buf
falo speech was the construction of the
isthmian canal, and the fourth was the
laying of a Pacific cable. To these four
"policies of good will and friendly trade
relations" President Roosevelt specifically
pledged himself in his declaration to the
Cabinet yesterday. No public announce
ment has yet been made as to details or
methods to be pursued In carrying put
these policies, but the President's insist
ence that he shall be aided by the men
who were to have assisted Mr. McKinley
in the same work is accepted as an assur
ance that there will be no radical depart
ure from the methods which would have
been followed had Mr. McKinley survived.
Secretary Hay Stays.
Secretary of State Hay declared to-day
that it was his purpose to retain the
portfolio originally thanded him by Presi
dent McKinley. He said it is hia inten
tion to stay as long as he feels capable
of satisfactorily attending to the "Taff airs
of. the Department of State. There was
no time limit on the invitation of Mr.
Roosevelt, nor has Hay put any time
limit with respect to his service in
Roosevelt's Cabinet. Hay pointed out to
a man with whom he talked that his
views are entirely in accord with those
of .Xfee President. Hay understands that
Roosevelt is completely informed of the
negotiations. with Great Britain with re
spect to the construction of the isthmian
canal, and has thoroughly concurred in
the policy adopted by President McKln
ley. Hay feels that he can still be of
usefulness and that it would be most un
gracious for a member of the Cabinet
to resign . after the President's request.
In 'the new administration he will con
tinue without change the negotiations by
which the United States will be freed
from the embarrassing obligations im
posed 'upon it by the Clayton-Bulwer
treaty. â¢ . -
It is true that prior to his election as
Vice President, Roosevelt strongly op
posed the position against fortifying the
canal, but since he assumed that office
he has been frequently consulted respect
ing negotiations, and- It is understood
that' there is no difference between his
and Hay's view for a neutral waterway.
Other Questions Pending-.
There are other questions pending which
Hay will proceed to dispose of under the
policy adopted before McKinley's assas
sination. The most important is that re
lating to reciprocity. Special Reciprocity
Commissioner Kasson will probably re
sume his duties this fall. He will continue
the tentative negotiations initialed last
spring and will have several conventions
in shape for signature immediately after
the ratification of French, British and
Argentine treaties still before the Senate.
"Hay will direct the signature of the
treaty for the cession of the Danish West
Indies to the United States and the treaty
of friendship and navigation and com
merce with Spain, and will take steos for
a revision of the treaties existing between
the United States and China. Absolute
observance of the cardinal principles
enunciated by Hay in, his cable note of
July 1, 1900, with respect to China will
continue. - -'-â¢ ' S =â¢ -Â¦ :.;->'
THIRTY THOUSAND PEOPLE VIEW BODY
A\ question which -will shortly arise with
respect to China will be that relating to
Manchuria, and both Roosevelt and Hay
are in accord in the determination tofully
protect American interests in that region.
The controversies between Canada and
the United States will be amicably ar
ranged, it is hoped, as McKinley desired,
but while willing to make concessions In
some other matters neither Roosevelt nor
Hay is prepared to authorize the cession
of a single foot of Alaskan territory to
Great Britain.

Apologizes to Cork Council.
CORK, Sept. 18.â At to-day's meeting of

Cave apologized for the reference he made
to the President McKinley last Fri
dav when he opposed a resolution ot

Americans Assemble in Berlin.
BERLIN, Sept. 18.â The American col
ony met at the United States Embassy
at 3 o'clock this afternoon to express sym
pathy and condolence on the death of Mr.
McKinley. General Schwan and many
ladies were present. Secretary Jackson
made the opening speech, and William E.
Curtis read an address in which the late
President's greatness, goodness and
kindness were referred to. Mr. Curtis
Dictured him as the workingman's friend,
and said it was incredible that he should
meet his fate at the workingman's hand.
The speaker condemned America, s na-r
tional liberality toward degenerates who
seek to destroy society and kill kings.

Arrival of the Train.
For a full minute after it had stopped

Tribute of Schoolgirls.
At one time a group of schoolgirls ap
proached the casket. There were six of
them and they, came three abreast. One
of the forward row leaned over for a Iook
and, gently disengaging rrom the bosom

were resting there. The others followed
her example, and although the sentries
had orders to permit nobody to place any
thing upon the coffin or to touch the
floral offerings that were already there,
the little tributes of the girls were al
lowed to remain, and they were still on
the top of the coffin when it was carried
through the door on its way to McKin
ley's cottage on North Market street. All

the catafalque,' approximately at the rate

in which the body lay in state a total of
30,000 people, practically a number equal
to the actual population of Canton.
When the doors were closed at 6 o'clock
the line, four abreastr stretched fully one
mile, from the Courthouse, and people
were coming from the side streets to take
their places in line.

Human Tide at the Bier.
As the people hurried by the bier they
gave expression to suppressed exclama
tions of anguish at the sight of the
pinched face lying there in the coffin. It
was not the face they had known so
well, and almost without exception the
people turned j away regretful that thay
had not retained in their minds that"pic
ture of manly vigor which they knew so
well. Many of his nearest friends re
frained from looking upon the face, pre
ferring to preserve the memory of him as
in life.
Late in the afternoon an aged man

managed with difficulty, appeared at the

allow 'him to enter, and when the sol
dier refused, saying that he had received

door, the old man stood back, the picture

the young sentry In pleading tones to al
low-him entrance through the doorway,
saying that in his feeble condition he was
not able to stand in line, which at that
time was extending fully a mile from the
entrance.

nHICAGO. Sept. IS.â Magistrate Prin
diville to-day decided to allow
Emma Goldman, the anarchist, her
freedom until the case comes up for
y hearing, under bonds of $20,000. Her
attorneys said they had secured
$15,000, and immediately left the
courtroom to seek the additional money
necessary. Meanwhile Miss Goldman was
led back to the women's annex at . the
Harrison-street station. She was visibly
disappointed at the amount of bond re
quired.
"I guess they thought they would make
the bond so big I could not furnish it,"
she said, "but I have friends the police
know nothing about, and I'll be out of
here by night."
The hearing of Miss Goldman's case on
the charge of "conspiracy to murder
President McKinley" was spt for to-mor
row. Her counsel, however, learning that
there would be no session of the court to
morrow because of the President's fu
neral, decided at a conference to take the
matter before a Magistrate to-day. The
matter of Mis3 - Goldman's guilt or in
nocence of the charge against her was not
mentioned, as Judge .Prindiville had de
cided to follow the example of Judge
Chetlain, who has the cases of the other
anarchists before him. â¢ Â¦
Mi3s Goldman wa3 exceedingly cheerful
when shs was brought into court by
Chief Matron. Keegan, and chatted vi
vaciously with her attorneys, Messrs. Sal
tiel, Geeting and Brown. . .
Mr. Geetmg, for Miss Goldman, ad
dressed the court first. He said that
while the defense was willing to allow
the police every opportunity to secure
evidence against Miss Goldman, -as well
as the other anarchists, he was confident
that Miss Goldman wag entitled -under
the law to "freedom, under bonds.
"I can assure this court," said the law
yer,' "that my client, if under no bond
at all other than her word, .would appear
before this court whenever desired.
, John E. Owens, representing the city
prosecutor, advanced no objection to ban
being allowed, but pleaded that because
of the importance of the case the bond
be made'as heavy as possible, in accord-
ance with the Illinois statutes. Miss Gold
man has not yet furnisied the bend.

"William McKinley was the choice
of the people of the United States
yfor President and the country is en
titled to have his policies carried
out as nearly as possible on Iine3
which would have been followed if "he
had lived."
This is the idea which President Roose
velt has had continually in mind ever
since Mr. McKinley's death. It prompted
his declaration on taking the oath that
the policies of the late President were to
be carried out. His first step was to in
sist upon each one of the late President's
advisers remaining in office. This insist
ence was so strong that no one of the
men who had aided - Mr. McKinley in
formulating his policies felt at liberty to
refuse to aid Mr. Roosevelt in carrying
them out. He also urged Secretary. Cor
telyou to remain in office, and It can Â¦ be
announced that Mr. Cortelyou will re
main and will give the new President the
benefit of his intimate knowledge of the
intentions of Mr. McKinley not only as to
broad policies but also as to appointment
to office, for in this respect, too, the inten
tions of the late President are to be car
ried out and promises that had been made
are to be fulfilled. " .. '

In none of the official reports so far
made does It appear that the secret ser
vice men took any part in the arrest,
though they demanded- that the prisoner
be turned over to them after he had been
disarmed. From photographs which have
been placed in the President's handÂ» the
secret service men appear more as mem
bers of the receiving party or as specta
tors than as officers on duty looking out
for dangers that threatened the life of
McKinley. t
President Roosevelt will Investigate the
bureau to determine whether the men
there employed are experienced detec
tives. Â¦ If the men are merely placeholders
the President will give the bureau a se
vere shaking up.
It appears from reports made to the
President that the secret service men who
were with McKinley were not men with
such experience as to be entrusted with
such duties. They were not detectives
acquainted with criminal methods, nor
were they alert in their duty as guards to
the President. They did not even accom
plish the arrest of the assassin, as ap
pears from the report of Captain. Wisser
of the Seventy-third Coast Artillery,
whose men weie on duty and disarmed
Czolgosz after the negro Parker had
struck him down.

MARSHFIELD, Or., Sept. 18.â John
Peterson, a foreigner, was run out of
Marshfield to-day on account of utter
ances derogatory of the late President
McKinley. A party led by business men
visited Peterson's residence last night
with the intention of administering a coat
of tar and feathers, but he refused to
come out and the attempt was abandoned.
This morning he came down town and
was immediately taken into custody by a
committee of citizens. â¢ Tar and feathers
were in readiness in a warehouse, but
the committee finally gave him one minute
to make his choice between tar and feath
ers and leaving the country within five
hours. He chose the latter. -;

"The hopeful view was taken, and quite
naturally it could be so explained. It
is easy to be wise after the event and to
say that in this respect the surgeons
were in error. Err they certainly did. as
the result shows, but to err in such a
way argues no incapacity or avoidable
Jack of judgment. It simply, we repeat.
Illustrates the fact that the medical man
is not a i>erfect being.
"Gangrene probably was established
two or three days before the fatal issue
followed, but it could hardly have oc
curred very early without giving rise to
more disquieting phenomena than aug
mentation of the pulse and respiration,
which, as we have said before, might
weJI have been due to some comparative
5jT unimportant disturbance. To the
yfround of the kidney we attribute little
importance further than arises from the
fact that it made one more traumatic
surface to become gangrenous. There Is
said to have been a trifling degree of
homatura of brief duration, but not
enough to indicate a very serious renal
"The case of the profoundly lamented
President may be set down as unique in
pome of its features, not so much, per-
J-aps, as regards the actual traumatism
inflicted by the assassin's bullet, as with
regard to the deferred appearance of tlie
gangrenous process that blotted out his
fair prospects of recovery." â¢
Doctors "Were in Error.
"The operation having been finished
without seriously taxing the distin
guished patient's vital powers, there fol
lowed at least five days of freedom from
serious symptoms. This we say with full
appreciation of the fact that the record
of the pulse and respiration teemed omi
nous, for the high rate might have been
due to any one of a number of conditions
not in themselves of grave importance.
"Without delay he was taken to a well
equipped hospital and attended by sur
geons of world wide reputation and vast
experience. The operation itself was per
formed by an exceedingly capable gyne
cologist, who was assisted by equally
capable surgeons. It is perfectly certain
that there was no technical fault in the
operation and it may be said with equal
positiveness that it would have verged
on madness to prolong the search for the
bullet after it had been acsertained that
it had not inflicted any very grave in
jury beyond that of the stomachâascer
tained, that is to say, within the limita
tions of warrantable efforts.
Operation Well Done.
"At the time of his assassination Presi
dent McKinley probably was in better
physical condition than most men of his
'age who lead a sedentary life. So far
as is known he Â¦was free from, all organic
disease, though his vitality may have
been somewhat impaired by the fearful
mental strain to which the duties of his
oSice and its responsibilities and anxie
ties had long subjected him. He was
suddenly cut down by a cruel wound,
but he bore it bravely, and there was
little of the condition known as shock.
This freedom from shock was correctly
interpreted as showing that no consider
able internal hemorrhage was going on.

The Board of Education held its regular
meeting yesterday but transacted no busi
ness out of reapect to the memory cf the
late lamented President McKinley. After
the adoption ot the following resolution
the meeting adjourned:
Whereas. Under the inscrutable dispensation
of Providence the nation stands to-day be
reaved through the death of Its late beloved
chief magixtrate, William McKinley; and
whereas, it is tre desire of this board to show
In every way possible its sorrow as .well as Its
respect for his memory. Be it -
Resolved, first. That all the public schools of
San Francisco be and they are hereby ordered
clcsed during; Thursday, September 19.
Second, That fasts shall be displayed at half
mast on every schoolhouse in San Francisco
from sunset of !3Â«ptember 18 to sunset of Sep
tember 19.
Third. That tliU board does now adjourn.
Postpone Golden Jubilee.
Despite the fact that announcements of
the postponement on account of tfca
death of President McKinley of the goldK
en Jubilee of the Dominican College at
San Rafael have been published, so many
invitations have been sent out that the
executive committee fears that some may
not receive word of the change. Many
costly presents have been sent to- the col
lege from former pupils now in Europe
as well as cash donations. These will be
asknowledged by the Sisters in charge.

SEATTLE, Sept. lS.-Valentine Gobel
attempted to commit suicide by swallow
ing laudanum on a west-bound Great
Northern passenger train last night near
Spokane. Two anarchistic pamphlets
containing seditious language were found
on his person. The United States secret
service is looking up Gobel'a record.

CITY OF MEXICO, Sept. 18.â The
Chamber of Deputies" of Mexico, by a
unanimous vote, decided to-day to sus
pend its session of Thursday, to drape its
tribunes in mourning for three days, to
cause the Mexican flag to be^. half-masted
over the Congress building for the' same
length of time and to send a message of
condolence to Washington. â¢ .
LIMA^ Peru, Sept. 18. â Yesterday the
United States Minister, Irving B. Dudley,
visited President Romana, the Senate, the
Chamber of Deputies, the Foreign Minis
ter, Senor Chacaltana; the Mayor of
Lima, and the various legations and ex
pressed the thanks of the American na
tion and Government for their sympathy
and cablegrams of condolence regarding
the death of President McKinley.

members of the diplomatic corps.
THE HAGUE, Sept. 18.â Dr. Kuyper,

, At a meeting of the General George G.
Meade Post, G. A. R.. in Pioneer building,
last Tuesday, the following resolutions in
memory of the departed comrade, Major
William McKinley, were unanimously
adopted: .
Resolved, That we deplore and sincerely
grieve over the death of our beloved com
rade. Major William McKinley, late President
of the United States.
Resolved, That we further deplore his un
timely taking: off and the dastardly method
by which his family, his country and his com
rades were deprived of his great counsels and
devotion. . .
Re-solved, That our comrade, William Mc-
Kinley, was a typical American as a citizen,
a soldier and a statesman; and that : no. Ameri
can was ever held in greater esteem by the
people of his country during "the period of his
high public career than was William McKin
ley. â¢ "
Resolved, That we deprecate and denounce
the means by .which he met his death, and
call urgently upon all honest Americans to
suppress by every lawful means the class of
people whose teachings have led to this great
national calamity. Â¦
Resolved That this country should not be
free enough to shelter 'anarchists from abroad,
nor should it be permitted to be the breeding
place of that class.
- Resolved, That the colors of the post be
draped in mourning for a. period of thirty
days.
Resolved, Also that a copy of the foregoing
resolutions be spread on the minutes of the
vest. JOHN MURPHY,' Commander.
WILLIAM F. HOWE. Adjutant.

Ignatian Council No. 35, Young Men's
Institute, adopted the following memo
rial resolutions by a rising vote at a re
cent meeting:
Whereas, The hand of a brutal assassin by
a cowardly blow has deprived our country of its
beloved President, and whereas, by the dread
calamity our nation has been robbed of an
able and conscientious leader, whose every ac
tion, both in public and private life, has been
characterized by the highest degree of sterl
ing integrity and uprightness, and whereas, by
his recent visit among us he had endeared
himself in particular to the people of Callfor,
nia, be it
Resolved, That we view with horror and
deep Indignation the great calamity which has
befallen our fair land, and that we condemn
in unmeasured terms the dastardly act, and
that we hereby desire to express our most pro
found and sincere sorrow over our nation's
afiliction, and that our -deepest sympathy goes
forth to the stricken wife and sorrowing rela
tives of pur late beloved President, and be it
further
Resolved, That we do now adjourn out of
resDect to his memory.
J. B. QUEEN. President.
C. L. EBNER, Secretary.
Members Adopt Resolutions Deplor
ing the Cowardly Act of the
Assassin.

To the widow of our comrade we extend our
deepest heartfelt sympathy. We fervently
hope the knowledge that she was his most
dearly beloved on earth will be such solace and
comfort to her that she will bear her sor
rowful bereavement with the courage and for
titude which has ever distinguished her, . and
which made her the support and helpmate of
her illustrious husband. _ Â¦
With awed and mournful submission to the
Inscrutable will of providence we here record
our sorrow and grief, and express' our convic
tion that the name of William McKinley will
ever live in the hearts of his people as that
of the best beloved American- of his time.
mourns with all America, this, terrible afflic
tion. We loved our noble comrade for the
goodness of his heart; for his lofty patriotism;
for the rectitude of his private life; for his
grand and sublime virtues, and for the abili
ties continuously manifested by him in the dis
charge of the arduous and Complicated duties
of his exalted station.
James A. Garfield Post No. 34, Department
of California and Nevada. G. A. R., deeply
The President is dead! Dead by ahe ruth
less hand of a cruel assassin; struck down In
the midst of his loving countrymen who proud
ly looked upon him as the personification of all
that is grood. grand and glorious in mankind.
His untimely death is a fearful blow to civ
ilization, and overwhelms the American peo
ple with an Irreparable loss.

The following letter was sent yesterday
to the late President's private secretary
by the Tliird Baptist Church of this city:
SAN FRANCISCO, Sept. 17, 1901.
Secretary Cortelyou, Canton, Ohio: The Third
Baptist Church (Afro-American) and its Chris
tian Endeavor Society . held special memorial
services on Sunday evening for our beloved
President. It was -there desired that convey
ance be made to Mrs. McKinley, through you,
of our sincere and heartfelt sympathy In this
hour of bereavement. In the passing of Presi
dent McKinley Christianity has lost an. earnest
worker and worthy advocate; our country, the
truest exponent of its national principles, and
the world one of Its noblest of men, whose
flawless character , will shine forth as an ex
ample and guide to humanity now and for
generations to come.
TVe mourn with you the death of our Pres
ident as an individual visitation into our house
holds of Him who said, "Come unto Me, all
ye that labor and are heavy laden, and I will
give you rest." We commend you, dear
madam, to His words, "Blessed are they that
mourn, for they shall be comforted." Tours in
loving sympathy, . .
REV. JAMES H. KELLY, Pastor.
MRS. B. L. TYRREL, Pres. C. E. Society.
MIPS M. GIBBS. Associate.
T. B. MORTON, Church Clerk..
Pastor and Congregation. Send Letter
of Condolence to Private
Secretary Cortelyou.

TKE Citizens' Committee of One
Hundred met in Mayor Phelan's of
fice yesterday to complete arrange
ments for the memorial service in
honor of the late President, to be
heid this afternoon at Mechanics*
Pavilion- Chairman F. W. Dohr
marm of the committee on invita
tion filed a report by which it is desired
to convey the information that the gen
eral public will be admitted on the Lar
kin street side. The doors will be opened
at 1:15 p. m. The report follows:
General admission of the public on Larkln
itreet side, for which no cards are necessary.
Invited officials are requested to enter the
Polk street side of the Pavilion (not es stated
on Invitation, on Hayes street), showing their
Invitations at the door. Invited officials will
be seated on ground floor on arrival, except
those who will occupy seats on the platform.
These are requested to form in groups, as des
ignated by signs in the anteroom, and will
then form into procession and take their allot
ted seats at 1:45 sharp, their ladies being seat
ed on the ground floor as they arrive. This
proeessitih will consist of the Mayor, the Gov
ernor, commandinc officer of the army, com
manding officer of the navy and the minister
and orator of the day, foreign consuls, judi
ciary, representatives of universities, members
of Senate and Congress, army and navy. Gov
ernor's staff, city officials, State officials. Na
tional Guard and other representative bodies.
The Grand Army and Loyal Legion will en
ter by the Polk street side in a body at 1:30
and march directly into the hall, where seats
â¢re specially reserved for them. No seats will
be reserved for invited officials after 2 o'clock
and no invited guests can be permitted to as
cend the platform after the exercises have been
opened.
The members of the chorus and the musicians
will be admitted by their, special tickets at
the Grove street entrance.
Owing to the very short time at the dls
posar of the committee and the difficulty of
getting invitations addressed, etc., the com
mittee hopes that any slight inaccuracies which
may have happened will be pardoned.
Holla V. Watt, chairman of the pro
gramme committee, announced that after
the funeral march the audience will sing
tv.-o verses of "America."
The Third Artillery Band will give a sa
cred concert at the Golden Gate Park
from 2:30 to 4:30 p. m. The original in
tention of having solemn mupic in front
of the City Hall to accommodate the
overflow at the Pavilion was abandoned.
Professor Spadina stated that as many
of the best musicians had been engaged
this afternoon for the Pavilion and for
the Oakland memorial exercises, another
eacred concert will be given at the park
on next Sunday.
The music platform in the Pavilion will
accommodate 640 singers and SO musicians.

The Board of Health made its annual
visit to the leper settlement at Molokai
last Saturday. Since the last visit a new
system has been put into operation to
prevent those who go to see relatives at
the settlement from having personal con
tact with the lepers. This time the visi
tors were kept in a stockade, apart from
the lepers, . and visiting relatives could
only talk to lepers they had come to see
through two fences four feet apart. ThÂ«
lepers were bitterly opposed to the inno
vation and some of them threatened to
break the stockade down.
Governor Dole, who is attending to busi
ness daily, has proffered encouragement
to the Hamakua planters to put forth
their utmost resources in quelling the
forest fires. If they furnish vouchers of
the expense to which the effort will sub
ject them the Governor engages to ask
the Legislature to pay the bills. Al
though Government forests are being
devastated, there Is no appropriation for
fighting flre.
A wireless dispatch from Maul reports
fifteen, acres of cane burned at Spreckels
ville. 'The cane will be ground immedi
ately, so that the loss will be slight. A
Japanese has been arrested for starting
the flre.
Just before the Mariposa sails news by
wireless telegraph from Hilo says that
recent strong winds have' caused the
Hamakua fire to break out afresh with
renewed fierceness. The flre Is in the
Government forest now but is back burn
ing and coming down the slope, threaten
ing homesteads abovo Ookala and Ku
kalau plantations.
There is but Httle hope of rain before
November, when the rainy season will be
due, according to normal conditions. The
drought in the Hamakua and Kohala dis
tricts is a calamity- Itself apart from flre.

Thomas Costello. a non-union teamster,
employed by Scott & Magner, hay and
grain dealers, secured a warrant from
Judge Cabaniss yesterday afternoon for
the arrest of four men on the charge of
assault to murder. Although he does not
know their names, he says they are strik
ing teamsters and he can easily identify
them. He was driving in a buggy along
Sixth street Tuesday afternoon and when
opposite Clara street four men dragged
him out 'of the buggy and beat him on
the head with a quart bottle, breaking the
bottle and severely lacerating his scalp.
P. Vahey and Albert Nelson, union sail

of battery was booked against them.
They were trying to compel sailors who
were unloading a vessel to quit work.

anil the demands of.the Htrlkern are
mo unreasonable that they- cannot
and will not be acceded to. .
A. large number of men who desire to work
may find permanent employment at Port Costa
at good wages and we recommend the farmers
throughout the State to select and send, suit
able men there for. that puroose. -
dissatisfaction with the wages or employement,
but they were restrained by the strike leaders
in San Francisco. They claimed that the
labor unions of San Francisco had helped them
In their successful strike In July for higher
wages and shorter hours. They requested this
committee to visit the leaders of the Water
Front Federation at San Francisco and ascer
tain If something: could not be done to release
them from the present strike. We also visited
the warehousemen and grain handlers of Port
Coeta and found they had about 350 men at
workâ abcat one-half the usual number so em
ployed at this season of the year unloading
cars and barges and loading ships.
These men are boarded and lodged at the
warehouses, on barges and In temporary struc
tures. They are working under protection of
Deputy SheriffÂ»-of Contra Costa County. We
ascertained that there were no men then at
work -who are boarded and lodged at any of
the hotels, and that the assaults that have
been committed are preventing: their belns so
entertalr.ed or their going: out on the street
and mingling with the citizens of, the town.
We are also Informed that the hotels refuse to
accommodate non-union laborers, but are en
tertaining men who are out on strike.
Violence and intimidation have seriously in
terfered with obtaining help necessary to
handle the usual amount of grain that should
be handled at this time of the year, and the
temporary accommodations for men employed
are said to be full.
.The business of handling this season's crop
is in a badly congested state.
We interviewed Sheriff Veale of Contra Costa
County, who stated that with proper assistance
from the Board of Supervisors he could handle
the situation and maintain absolute order, but
that up to the present time such assistance
has not been granted him; that until euch as
sistance Is given him the present state of af
fairs must continue. He assured your commit
tee that he would deputize any number of
reputable citizens of the State who might be
cent there to preserve law and order and move
the crops. Unless men are sent the necessary
portion of the crop cannot be handled In time
to prevent serious loss.
Tour committee then visited San Francisco
and at the request of the Port Costa strikers
called on Mr. Furuseth, strike manager of the
"Water Front Federation. Wo stated to him
that we had Interviewed the strikers at Port
Costa and that they were ready to go to work
provided his consent could be obtained. We
explained to him thÂ« unprotected condition of
the crops throughout the State and the fact
that they must be moved at once. We told
him that the farmers would be glad to have the
union men handle their crops If they would, but
that unless they did the rarmers themselves
would be compelled to do the work to prevent
serious loes. Mr. Furuseth said that he would
not consent to that, that he would release no
one. He advised us to call on the executive
committee of the Employers' Association of
San Francisco and get that body to accede to
the demands of the striking teamsters of that
City. . . i
We then visited some members of the execu
tive committeÂ« of the Employers' Association
and were eiven a full account of the causes
leading to the organization of that body and the
work It was doing. We believe the position
taken and maintained by that organization to
be reasonable and Justifiable.
We herewith submit and append to this re
pott a copy of the declaration of principles of
the Employers' Association, and also a copy of
an article of agreement which the strikers de
manded the business men of San Francisco to
sljm. They are' submitted for your considera
tion and comparison: The presentation and
demand for the signing of such articles of
agreement is one of the causes for the existing
labor troubles.
Mr. Finkle is at present the chief civil
and hydraulic engineer of the Edison
Electric Company and the California
Power Company of Los Angeles. He was
for some years consulting engineer of the
three electric.companies of Redlandsr and
was promoted to his present position on
the occasion of the recent consolidation
of all the electric companies in the south
ern metropolis. He is a prominent mem
ber of the Order of Elks and of other fra
ternal organizations.

vies. chief inspector of the San Francisco
Gas and Electric Company. She is very
popular among a large circle of friends
and Is the possessor of a fine soprano
voice. .

When the Summers came to Seattle a
year ago from Butte, Mont., they were
without money. They at once began mak
ing the acquaintance of the Catholics of
the city, with lhe result that they were
soon employed on the Catholic Progress.

were in Portland, however, on the 10th
inst., for they induced a Mr. Hess to cash
a draft,, which was sent to the National
Bank of Commerce here for collection. It'
was drawn on the Catholic Progress and
was indorsed by Mrs. Summers, but, of
course, Mr. Bookmyer pronounced it

Special Dispatch to -The Call.
"A woman came to me yesterday with
a pitiable story of the mistreatment of
her husband by the strikers. He is a
porter and he went out with his union.
After a while he could endure no longer
to see his family starving: and he Â¦went
to work again for his former employer.
The strikers lay in wait for him and beat
him most cruelly. He is now nearly re
covered and is anxious to get to work,
but mortal fear assails him whenever he
grets twenty feet from his own door. His
wife intended to go to the- newspapers
and tell their story, but she was given
to understand that if she did or if her
husband went back to work the strik
ers will retaliate upon the whole family.
Site came down at once and begged of
me not to give her name to the report
ers."
"lhe dissatisfied strikers are being kept
in line not merely by the fear of attacKs
and brutal beating's. They are given to
understand that they will be killed if
they do not stand by the union. Fear
of that is what keeps many from going
back to worlr.
'â¢The record of the hospitals and City
Prison show the situation to be most out
rageous. And yet I do not believe that
more than one assault in ten gets into
the papers or is recorded at the Jail or
hospitals. A non-union man is attacked
and beaten and left half dead. Possibly
he is able to go home, or friends help
him heme. His injuries are dressed by
nis wife or if more serious bv his physi

One of the curious things about the assassination
of the President is a number of persons cropping- up
in all parts of the country who had heard reports of
a conspiracy to commit the crime but who never
thought of saying anything about it.
It;is not often that outsiders say â¢ better things
about us' than we say about ourselves, but sometimes
they do. For example, the Atlanta . Constitution
says: "The Merchants' . Association of San Fran
cisco has broken the power of the evil and unclean
gangs and placed the city on a healthy foundation."
It will be" perceived that while the report is correct
as to the intent, it is rather premature as to the ac
complishment. Â¦ â¢ ..

visiting the Temple of 'Music at the^Buffalo expo
sition out of curiosity has given the show something

moner he admits receiving a book entitled, "Ellen,
or the Whisperings of an Old Pine," and apologizes
for having in a previous number made the word
"pine" read "pipe." It is hardly necessary to add
that he attributes the wrong to a typographical error.
On the other hand the objectors declare that to ac
cept a gift of the kind would be to make the Metro
politan a receiver of stolen goods, knowing them to
have been stolen. The Springfield Republican urges
a further objection in the statement: "To a nice
conscience these treasures of the consummate skill
of great Chinese artists for famous centuries will al
ways remain tainted not . with the stain of pillage
alone, but with the disgrace and blood of - innocent
; beings slain."
The officials of the museum are reported to be
quite sure that an art museum can properly be made
the custodian of lost, provided the loot have an ar
tistic value, and provided further that there be a way
of eliminating the word loot from consideration of
the problem. Thus the curator is quoted as saying:
"The collection cannot now be called loot, since it
has been put up at auction. It has had a commercial
experience." Another defender of the rightfulness
of accepting the donation says that art collections
made in that way are not novelties. It recalls the
fact that Napoleon looted the galleries and the
churches of nearly all Europe for the purpose of en
riching the great collections at the Louvre; that the
British Museum contains certain marbles of . the
Parthenon which Elgin bought from the Turks, who
looted. them from Athens; and, finally, many of the
great monumental works of art in Rome were looted
from subject cities. * '
.There is no question, of legality involved in the
issue. It is conceded that the gentleman who offers
the articles to the museum has a legal right to them.
He did not himself loot them. He purchased them at
auction in China from persons who had obtained them
by the simple process of taking them. The collection
is said to be large enough to fill several railway cars
and includes many valuable porcelains, bronzes,
wood carvings, ivories, paintings and silks. That
they would constitute an important exhibit in any
collection of oriental art is not denied. The one ob
jectionable feature about them is that they would
be looked upon by some persons as an exhibit of loot
rather than as a exhibit of art.
The cause of the discussion is that the articles con
tained in the gift are parts of the spoil that was ob
tained in the looting of Chinaâ that is to say, the
gift is virtually made-up of stolen property, and the
debate turns upon the moral right of the museum to
accept gifts of that kind.
AS a means of relieving their minds from the
perplexities of the problem of organizing a
combination strong enough to assure a tri
umph for honesty in the approaching city election
the people of New York have undertaken a discus
sion of the relation of morals to art. It is not an
academic discussion merely, for it relates to a gift
which has been offered to the Metropolitan Museum
of Art in that city; r.or yet is it strictly a practical
question, for it is well known what the issue will
be. In feet, it is. just the kind of a question most
likely to give rise to spirited i'tcbate .without com
pelling aijy one to commit himself decidedly one
way or the other.
It is certainly a curious anomaly in our law that
while it. makes such careful provision for the protec
tion of mail carriers it ignores the dangers that sur
round the Presidency, and it is well nigh certain that
at the coming session of Congress some effort will
be made to provide by Federal law for the punish
ment of persons who assail the President and the
chief officers of the Government
The section in the Federal statute, which in this
connection is sufficiently interesting to be quoted in
full, reads thus: "Section 5472â Any person who
shall rob any carrier, agent or other person intrusted
with the mail, of such mail, or any part thereof, shall
be punishable by imprisonment at hard labor for not
less than five years and not more than ten years;
and if convicted a second time of a like offense, or if
in effecting such robbery the first time the robber
shall wound the person having custody of the mail,
or put his life in jeopardy by the use of dangerous
weapons, such offender shall be punishable by impris
onment at hard labor for the term of his natural life."
Despite the earnestness of those who favor drastic
laws upon the subject, the general tone of the discus
sion has been adverse to such measures. The people
are not yet willing to admit that the President of the
republic needs any other provision for his safety than
that which the law affords to every citizen. The dis
cussion, however, has brought to light a curious
anomaly in our statutes in that respect. The Bos
ton Transcript directs public attention to the fact
that under the New York law had the President sur
vived the . maximum penalty which could have been
inflicted upon the assassin would Â¦ have been ten
years' imprisonment, while under the Federal statutes
a similar assault upon a mail carrier would have been
punishable by imprisonment for life.
WHEN it was believed that the President
would recover from his wound and that the
assassin would therefore under the law of
New York escape the death penalty, there was a
good deal of discussion concerning the proper
method of dealing with persons who the
assassination of a President of the United States.
Some authorities declared in favor of drastic meas
ures. Ex-Attorney General Griggs, for example, is
quoted as having recommended the passage of an
act making an attempt upon the life of the President
or any of the hjgher officers of the Government a
capital offense, whether it succeed or not, and pro
viding punishment by imprisonment for life for any
person convicted of- being a member of an organi
zation which teaches the murder of public officials.
ART AND MORALS.
The day of poison in the mental food of this peo
ple is over. They are using tongs to cast the Exam
iner into the gutter, where the scavenger will find it
and carry it away to defile the garbage with which
it rides :n his cart and rests upon the dumps. Tol
erance for it and those who make it what it is is at
tn end. Patience with its gorilla lusts, baboon mal
ice and monkey-shines is exhausted, and nothing it
may do will sugar and shine it to a disguise that may
jerrr.it its entry again into decent company. -â *; ;
San Francisco is in sackcloth' and ashes. Grief for
the dead is in the lines of all good men's faces and
in the hearts of all good women. But mingled with
it is a shame that downcasts- every eye when it is
remembered that here Hearst journalism took root,
tried its 'prentice hand and spread hence like a pes
tilence. Here the mangy pup gnawed its first bone,
did its first snapping and snarling at private charac
ter, yip'ped its earliest ululations against law and or
der and grew to be a dog bad enough and big enough
to invade any fold at night, cut any throat with its
fangs, and seek the blood of the best: â¢ But San
Francisco is repenting of her . indifference to the
growth of this beast and will cleanse her hands of
further responsibility for its existence.
The era of such impudence is over. The light of
exposure broke too late to save the life of a President
who has fallen, the most distinguished human sacri
fice of all time, to the greed, malice and venom of a
school of journalism which, with the name of its
founder, will be cremated in the fires of patriotic
wrath. . .
The Press now keenly analyzes the effect of the
notoriety that Hearst sought and that Mr. Cleveland
refused to be used in promoting.
WILLIAM R. HEARST: I decline to permit my sor
row for the men who died on the Maine to be .perverted
Into an advertisement for the Xew York Journal.
GROVER CLEVELAND.
One man whom he attempted .to use as a bill
board struck back at him a blow with the impact of
a cannon-ball. Hearst chose the corpses of the ten
score men who died on the Maine as an advertise
ment and organized a. movement to collect money to
erect to them a New York Journal monument. As
his vice presidents in this attempt he even secured
such men as Levi P. Morton and William C. Whit
ney, or said he had. He then wrote to ex-President
Cleveland for consent to use his name as a Journal
vice president. The keen intelligence, courage and
manhood of Mr. Cleveland saw through the wretched
scheme, and he answered in this curt note, which
should be framed and set over the desk of every pub
lic man in the United States:
Every event that focused public interest was seized
upon by Hearst, not for legitimate newspaper use,
but for special advertising of himself. His hired liars
gave Ananias the right to a new trial by their fakes
and inventions. In this way he published faked in
terviews with the Pope, the Queen Regent of Spain
and Mrs. McKinley. These were absolute inven
tions. No such interviews were given. His hirelings
never reached the presence of Pope, Queen or Presi
dent's wife. But Hearst wanted a reputation for en
terprise that would advertise him, and so the inter
views were invented and published. His offenses of
that kind against truth, decency and good conscience
were legion, and the exposure of one only stimulated
the inventio\i of another. By simulating interest in
some public question he would induce public men of
reputation to appear in his papers over their own sig
nature, only to characterize them as his "commis
sioners," or members of his staff, and put them in the
humiliating position cf sandwich men, toting his yel
low advertisement. All such as were so used by him
feel now keenly that they were being used to give
respectability and quasi-indorsement to the riot, an
archy and murder taught in his noisome papers.
' I "Â»HÂ£ New York Press, whose editor was threat-
I ened by Hearst in an attempt to make that
*â¢ paper desist from its exposure of the crime
breeding course of Hearst's papers, has laid down a
rule for the extinction of such journalism as Hearst
has originated and practiced. The remedy is prop
erly stated to lie with the people, by ignoring the cul
prit and his papers and refusing to be used as sand
wich men to advertise both.

YELLOW ADVERTISING.
iB Headlining and introducing an editorial
I from the New York Journal in our edition
firf yesterday an error was made by
cnarging that its publication occurred on
September 1 3, while the President was dy
ing. This was a mistake, occurring by haste
arm inadvertence, and due to the fact that
the New York Journal editorial was repro
duced in the Commercial-Tribune of Cincin
nati on September 1 3. We desire to be just
even to the unjust. The editorial was pub
lished in the Journal a few weeks prior to the
assassination of the President and is re
garded by public opinion as one of the in
fluences leading to that event, and was not
concurrent with the accomplishment of the
purpose which it seemed to express.
A CORRECTION.
In lineage the dead President was the last of his line. He sleeps beside his dead.
His father and his mother and his children rest by .his side, and the leaves of autumn
strew the place of their repose. But the high example of his lofty life leaves a lineage
that death cannot destroy and its inspiration will be as immortal as the republic.
This is a day of significance. Its tears will be dried, its somber trappings and all
its pomp and circumstance of solemn pageantry will pass away, but history will linger
long and fondly upon the story of which it is the last chapter. -
Life at its best is brief. Men bear their burdens for a day only and: the load falls
and its carrier is not. But as compared to the longest life and all that it may achieve,
how very brief those years in which his greatest work was done, but how eternal its im
pression!
The history of that brief space, between March 4, 1897, and the moment that his
heart ceased to beat, covers more of human tragedy, of the sport and change of fortune,
the alternation of storm and sunshine, the uplifting of a people from want and idleness
to plenty and occupation, than passed before in the history of man. In that timelthe
shell of the past burst and a new world issued fullfledged; and he saw it; all, and -was 'it~3
conscious impulse. He impressed all people with some expression of his genius, which
they wear as their password to a better life. Far China salutes his ' memory, as the
Just Man, and those whom he delivered as the Man of Power. ; <
From his high place he ministered through 1 'wise administration to the materialities
of his countrymen, and was not only commander in chief of his country's army' and
navy, but of his country's commercial conquest of the world. When he first uttered the
great oath which obligates a President, chaos was upon industry and labor was on famine
rations. When from his death bed he looked his last upon the beautiful trees plenty was
bursting the storehouses and piled every table.
- Â¦Â¦-. - Â¦Â«._.-Â¦ | â¢ ....... â¢..-.,
TO-DAY the last rites are observed and the mortal presence of the dead President
passes from sight, through that narrow door that at last closes upon all human
ity. While standing on the dome of his career there passed before him a paro
rama of events *that stirred his country to the depths. For the second tinie he
heard the call to arms. ' When the first sounded it thrilled his boyish heart and nerved
him to spring from obscurity into history. The second time his own hand rang the
tocsin, and in response thousands left the furrow and the forge, like him emerging into
the trials and sufferings of the larger life and risk, to which the ineffable inspiration of
patriotism calls men and they come heedless of restraint. The survivors of the Civil War
heard the bugle call once more, and Union and Confederate soldier alike, forgetting their
battles and differences, saluted the old flag and marched side by. side to vindicate the
majesty of their country and its institutions. He saw the last embers of civil strife and
sectional hate smolder and die, and the vision" of a reunited people' become reality. He
saw the empire of Philip II die, swathed in all its sins, and its epitaph blazoned with all
its virtues. He saw the dream of that world-grasping monarch fade in the sunshine of
an awakened world and the proud estate of its Emperor probated to the republic.
He felt the thrill that was upon us as our regiments marched away and the pall that
fell upon our spirits as the helpless dead came back to their narrow homes.

The Rev. Henry Codman Potter was
born in Schenectady, N. T., in 1835, and
was educated for the priesthood in Phil
adelphia. His rise in the Episcopal
church was rapid and his ability so ap
parent that in 1887 he was consecrated
Bishop of the New York diocese, the
largest in the United States.
It seems more than lively that the fa
mous Bishop, Right Rev. Henry Codman
Potter, will be elected to the primacy of
the church. Bishop Potter is a descend
ant of a noted family of divines. Hte
father, Alonzo' Potter, was for twentyi
years Bishop of Pennsylvania.

Turf Congress is a thing of the past.
Representatives of the seven associations
comprising the organization met in tho
final annual session to-day. After a few
minutes' session It v/as voted to disband
the organization, which is superseded by
the Western Jockey Club. It was decided
that the "Western Turf Association of San
Francisco was not a member of the con
gress, as it had not lived up to the ru!e3
requiring annual race meetings.

Jere Dunn- protested against Leonora

the Great Filly stakes by Major G. B.

NEW YORK, Sept. 18.â The stewards of
the Coney Island Jockey Club to-day sus
tained the protest of , Jere Dunn against
the fllly, ' Leonora Lorinff, winner of the
Great Filly stakes. They disqualified her-
Much interest was taken at the Graves
end track to-day when the decision of the
stewards became known. The decision
gives the rich stake of $24,000 to William
C. Whitney, whose filly, Blue Girl, was
beaten a short head after a questionable
ride by Shaw, for which ha was suspend
ed three racing days. The matter must
now be submitted to the stewards of the
jockey club for their approval.

SAN JOSE, Sept. lS.-An effort Is to be
made by. a number of fruit growers to
cause a dissolution of tho California Cured
Fruit Association. At a meeting of the
prune men to-day it was decided that the
association had not achieved the success
contemplated and that the best Interests
of the fruit industry demanded that the
affairs of the association be concluded.
About forty orchardists were present.
The dissolution of the association will be
undertaken, as provided for in its consti
tution. This is by consent of two-thirds
of: the members of the association and the
appointment of three receivers, to whom
will be entrusted the liquidation of the
concern. . . , ,

Eastern Baseball.
It is believed Wright's mangled arm
will have to be ampuated. One of his
ears was torn off and he was otherwise
bruised. . Â¦ -
Again and again he was carried around
with the belt, and each time tried to re
cover the knife. He finally succeeded,
and grasping the blade cut the belt and
secured his release.
At each revolution his arm was crushed,
but Wright did not lose his presence of
mind. Remembering that he had a knife
In his left pocket of his trousers, he
struggled desperately to get It, reaching
across hi3 body with his right hand. Just
as he was about to use It ' the knife
dropped from his grasp to the floor.
SACRAMENTO, Sept. 18. â Thomas
Wright, an employe at the Red Stone
mine at Blue Canyon, Placer County,, Is
undergoing treatment In the Sisters' Hos
pital in this city after a thrilling experi
ence. Wright had been placed in charge
of the machinery, of the mine during the
absence of the engineer. In some manner
his left arm became caught in the large
belt, and he was lifted from the ground
and carried around the shaft at tremen
dous speed.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
A miner, named McMurtle on Gulnn
Mountain is reported to have been badly
burned while trying to rescue his ef
fects from his burning cabin, but it is not
known whether he â¢ Is fatally burned.
Quigley & Co.'s sawmill at the foot of
Woodland Mountain with 'the settlement
surrounding it has been burned.
The fire started % on Woodland Mountain
and has spread to Guinn and Bryan
mountains and Is still burning' fiercely.
The Ios3 from the destruction of timber
will be many thousands of dollars, and
will be almost irreparable to the miners
operating in that district. Many head of
cattle are reported to have perished.
DENVER, Col., Sept. 18.â A special to
the Republican from. Eldora, Boulder
County, Col., says that town is seriously
threatened by a forest fire which started
on Woodland Mountain Sunday. All the
inhabitants of the town . are remaining
up to-night to nght the fire should it
reach there. Already thirty-flve square
miles of heavy timber has been burned
and many mine buildings were destroyed.
These are supposed to include the build
ings of the Revenge mine, where fifteen
men are on duty to protect the property.
The telephone from Eldora to the Re
venge mine has gone down and it Is not
known whether the fire has reached there
or not. Quite a settlement surrounds tho
mine and all the families with their
household goods were removed to Eldora
to-day. Three women and one man who
were the last to leave the place were
cut oft from Eldora by the fire and had to
take ar-other route. Great fears are en
tertained for their safety.

A TESTIMONIAL concert will ba
given to P. A. Dunworth to
morrow evening In Odd Fellows'
Hall by the Columbia Minstrel
Company. The programme "ar
ranged for the entertainment Is an excel
lent one. Dorothy Studcbaker will sing,
and such well-known artists as William
McDonald, Eddie Sweeney, Jack Holland,
Lloyd Spencer, W. W. Brackett, Elton
Lambert, Oscar Franks, Charlie Oliver, F.
C. Hotallng, Nevlll Calish and Forrest &
Montague will take part.
Mr. Dunworth Intends leaving for the
East shortly and will make his debut on
the professional stage. . He possesses a
lyric tenor voice and Is extremely popu
lar. As a member of the Columbia Min
strel Quartet 'his voice has been heard to
advantage. He is a Native Bon and has
frequently sung In entertainments to aid
charitable Institutions.
Friends of the singer feel" confident that
Mr. Dunworth will achieve a success in
his profession, as he has a pleasing pres
ence and a well-trained voice.

SUMMARY.
Ruiz on Saul of Tarsus beat the others
away In the closing six furlong sprint,
and at odds of 3 to 1 was never headed.
Mike Strauss led Mike Rice out for the
second place, with Jim Hale, the favorite",
unplaced.
Foncon landed the fifth race at five fur
longs with Kitty Kelly. The mare opened
a 3 to 2 favorite, receding to 5 to 2. Fon
con took matters quietly to the stretch,
where he passed Nullah and Clarando,
getting in first by a length. Howson on
Gold Baron nabbed Clarando the last few
yards, finishing second. Nullah closed a
6 to 5 choice.
As Ransch had the leg up on Ringmas
ter and tlie company seemed common
place, the old stager was installed favor
ite for the mile and a furlong affair. He
raced out in the lead for a mile with
McFarlane, and then struck his colors.
"Chub" Russell sneaked along on the
outside with Kastalne the last sixteenth,
winning easily. Ashby on El Mido moved
thirteen minutes late and then took the
place. '
After besting the Burns & Waterhouse
entry, Elfonse, a head for the opening
sprint, Mamie Hildreth, piloted by Ruiz,
was disqualified, and the backers of
Rarjsch were made happy. Mamie Hil
dreth made the running, but when col
lared by Elfonse in the stretch, Ruiz, as
he always does, resorted to questionable
tactics and was rightfully set back. The
decision gave second money to Darlene
and the show to Pegalong.
Outclassing the other three starters In
the- six furlong purse run, Meehanus made
a runaway race of it. Rollick finished well
up, defeating Sir Dougal for third posi
tion.
The brown horse McKenna with Ward
behind him had first call in the pit when
odds were posted on the trot. Joe Selby,
driven by Nelson, beat him for the first
heat. This must have Â¦ been ' a' mistake.
He toyed with Selby in the next three.
Lucy G and Lady Granard appeared out
of their class.- While being warmed up
previous to the second heat Joe Selby
reared up, and falling- backward, gave
Driver Nelson a severe Jarring. The
horse's groom next took the reins and
was likewise treated. The veteran "Billy"
Donathon then got up in the sulky, fin
ishing the race.
A trotting event that pleased the old
timers and awakened remlnlscenses from
the time "of Brian Bora down to the birth
of the pnumatlc tired sulky and five run
ning recitals attracted another large
crowd to the Golden Gate race meeting
"yesterday at Oakland track. * Nothing of
a startling nature characterized the sport,
unless it was the partially successful at
tempt of Joe Selby. a starter In the 2:24
trot, to flatten out two of his drivers by
falling backward and rolling upon them.
Elfonse, Meehanus and McKenna. were
the successful favorites, and Ringmas
ter, Nullah and Jim Hale â¢ the disap
pointments. There will be no racing to
day.

From advices received in this city yes
terday it is definitely known that the ac
tion taken by General Chaffee in regard
to the two firms named was only a pre
paratory step, and that some very sensa
tional developments are sure to follow.
The island of Samar is looked upon as
the hotbed of the insurgents. A civil gov
ernment was established there Â¦ but was
found to be a failure owing to the law
less state of the island, and martial law
had. to be continued. â¢ !
The merchants "whose agents have been
expelled from the island of Samar com
prise two of the richest firms in Manila,
with branch offices in London. Though
they were known to Be aiding and abet
ting: the insurgents by buying hemp of
them and selling them rice and munitions
of war no. steps had been taken against
them in the past, the American Govern
ment being afraid that international, com
plications with England might follow.
Now that General Chaffee has ignored
the protests of the British Consul and
has indorsed the action of General
Hughes it is known that the officials^in
Manila will be rupported by the authori
ties in Washington in restraining mer
chants, whether German, English, Spaji
ish or any other rationality, from trading
with the insurgents or having any'com
munication with them.
The telegraphic news published yester
day announcing that General Hughes had
expelled the agents of Smith & Bell and
Warner & Barnes from the island of Sa
mar is followe-1 up by interesting news
received in this city.'

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time; the
fourth time column gives the last tide of the
day, except when there are but three tides, as
sometimes occurs, i The heights given are in
addition to the soundings on the United States
Coast Survey charts, except -when a minus ( â )
sign precedes the height, and then the number
given is subtracted from the depth given by
the charts. The plane of reference ia the mean
of the lower low waters.

Thomson from D. M. Thomson for deser

Lewis for extreme cruelty.

Pessy Goldstein on a charge of desertion

ARRIVAL OF THE MARIPOSA.
Oceanic Wail, Company's Flier Gets
in From Hawaii.
The Oceanic Steamship Company's.Mar
iposa arrived from Honolulu at an early
hour yesterday morning:. It was the in
tention of the management to retire her
several months ago, on the occasion of
her one hundredth trip, but owing to the
Btrlke the Alameda was not ready for
service, so the Mariposa had to keep on
the run.- On her arrival yesterday she
completed her one hundred and first
round trip, thus making a record that has
never been beaten by an American steam
ship. Captain Horace Z. Howard, the
popular general manager of the Oceanic
Steamship Company, was the Mariposa's
first master, and Captain Dowdell, now
of the Zealandla, was chief officer. Then
Captain Hayward took command and aft
er him Captain Houdlette. Captain Ren
nie, the present popular master, succeed
ed Houdlette. In the history of the Pacific
Ocean there has never been two more
consistent performers than the Alameda
and the Mariposa. and the traveling pub
lic will hail with delight their going back
into commission again rejuvenated.
The cabin passengers on the Mariposa
were the following named: <
K. C. Baldwin. George C. Beckley Jr., H. G.
Cooper and wife, C. M. Clark. A. H. Canby,
Miss L. A. Curtis, Hon. W. H. Cornwall. M.
Duffy, C. .A. de Cew, A. Enos and son,' J. S.
Espey, J. H. Estes, : A. Fries, Mr. Greenwood,
Jean Gerardy. C. C. Hopkins. B. G. Holt, Rev.
C. A. Hyde, Mrs. Hyde and child. Miss M.
Hitchcock, Miss M. Jones. C. H. Johnson, G.
Johnson, E. L. Kruss, N. Lakusta, C. I. Lewis,
Mrs. McQuade and maid. Miss McQuade, W.
E. T. Neumann, William Norton. Mrs. A.
Ormsby. Miss H. E. Ormsby, L. F. Prepcott,
R. E. Peake, W. J. Quanane, M. Rosenbladt,
Miss M. Reynolds, H. A. Spencer, Mrs. J.
Schumann. Miss Schumann, Hon. W. O. Smith,
L. K. Smith, Mr. Schnack, Clement G. Smith,
W; Thompson, F. Wheyman, H. F. Wichman.
Jean Gerardy Is the world-famous 'cello
player, who Is on his way from the Anti
podes to New York. A. H. Canby is his
manager. 0

The great singer is reported to be in
magnificent health, .and her many ad
mirers will warmly welcome her reap
pearance in this city.
THE music lovers of San Francisco
will be delighted to hear that
. Mme. Schumann-Heink, one- of
the greatest living contraltos, will
positively appear here this winter
with the Maurice Grau company at the
Grand Opera-house.
Alfred Bouvier, who represents Mr.
Grau in- this city, received yesterday a
telegram from him stating that Mme.
Schumann-Heink had been engaged for
another season. This^'talented vocalist
made a profound impression on her last
appearance during the grand opera sea
son in this city last winter. After leaving
here she went direct to Bayreuth, In Ger
many, and appeared with great success
during the Wagnerian opera season. She
is' still in Europe, but will shortly leave
,-for America and will come direct to this
â¢city for the opening of the grand opera
season on November 11.
LONDON. Sept. IS.â An alarming situa
tion exists at Grimsby, where a mob- of
locked-out fishermen this afternoon at
tacked and wrecked and- set fire to the
new offices of the Owners* Federation,
â¢where the owners of the fishing: fleet were
holding a meeting. The owners escaped
and the fire was extinguished, but the
police were powerless to quell the riot.
A strike has been in progress among the
fishermen at Grimsby for several months.
Four hundred steam fishing boats are
Idle and thousands of fishermen and
their families have been suffering great
hardships. Food had to be sent to them
by sympathetic committees.

Walker was last seen talking with the
storekeeper about the supplies for the
next day. An hour afterward, on an
alarm given by the storekeeper, the ship
was searched and no trace of the steward
was found. He had been previously talk
ing about jumping overboard, and no
other explanation can be given for his
disappearance.
When it became evident that the stew
ard had taken his own life it was with
difficulty that the storekeeper could be
restrained from sharing- the 'same fate.
Doctors were called and the man was
given treatment for his mental trouble.
VANCOUVER. B. G. Sept 18.-T. W.
"Walker, chief steward of the steamship
Empress of India, which arrived hero
early this morning from the Orient, is
supposed to have committed suicide dur
ing: the night while the vessel was com
ing up from Victoria to this port. Walker
had been suffering from mental aberra
tion all the way across on this run. He
was much in company with Robert
Blythe, the storekeeper, who seems to
have been similarly afflicted and who has
taken Walker's disappearance much to
heart.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
F. Abreu, who came up on the Grant, is
a wealthy Filipino. A nephew of his and
two grandsons are now being educated in
the United States, and Mr. Abreu has
come here to see how they are getting
along. He is a man of the world and
hopes great things for his country be
cause of its annexation to the United
States. * -

The Grant brousrht the following named
passengers from Manila to Nagasaki:
The Grant brought home 115 cabin pas
sengers, eleven becond cabin passengers,
233 civilians, eight convalescents, 580 en
listed men, twenty-nine deported men and
one stowaway. All told she brought home
Â»77 peopie. 'rne cabin passengers were tne
following named:
The United States army transport Grant
arrived from the Philippines yesterday.
She was a day behind her last run, but
on this occasion she was not "making
time," while last July she had to be here
in order that the Government might keep
faith with the volunteers who signed for
two years' service in â¢ the Philippines.
While running under "rush" orders the
Grant covered C542 knots in twenty-two
days four hours and two minutes, while
on this occasion it took her twenty-three
days lour hours and twenty-five minutes
to cover 6544 knots. A variation of only
two knots in such a run goes' to show
that Captain Bruguiere knows the cur
rents of the Pacific Ocean and also the
variations of the tides. Chief Engineer
LIndley says :he transport could easily
have reached San Francisco last Tuesday
evening- had he not been bound down to a
certain number of revolutions by the Gov
ernment.

The Metropolitan is the largest Industrial Company in the country, being aa
large as all the other companies put together.
It has over $62,000,000 of assets,, over $3,000,000 of surplus; nearly 5% millions of
Industrial Policies in force for an insurance ax&mnting to nearly a thousand mil-
lions of dollars.
It paid over $S,000,000 In death claims last year and pays a claim on the aver-

cents for a very small cake of it.MOICTREAI/, Sept IS.â The Duke and
Duchess of Cornwall and York Journeyed
from Quebec to Montreal to-day and were
greeted with another affectionate demon
stration from the people of Canada. They
departed from Quebec at 9:30 o'clock in
the morning and the last sound that fell
upon their ears was a cheer from the peo
ple. They had spent the night aboard
the roval yacht Ophir ana came ashore
shortly after 9 o'clock. The sumptuous
special train provided especially for their
lonsr Journey to the Pacinc Coast was in
waiting and as soon as they were aboard
it went epeedlng down tbe north shore of
the St. Lawrence on the road to Montreal.
The royal special was preceded half
an hour by another special train, which
carried Lord Mlnto, Governor General;
Sir Wilfrid Laurier. Premier, and mem
bers of the Dominion Cabinet. The peo
ple came for miles to give a cheer or wave
a flag at the fleeting train. . "-

reached at 3 o'clock, was an ovation.
Thousands of visitors had crowded into
the city to reinforce the residents and
thev all seemed to be massed In the neigh
borhood of Place Vigor station and the

Captain Harry Curzon was the flrst to
grasp his old comrade's hand. "When we
left Negros I was the last of the regiment
to say good-by to him," said Curzon.
"You see, I w-is his adjutant." After that

as the sun, whioh hitherto was burled in

DUKE HONORS
WAR VETERANS
The tug's Governor Markham and Gov- |
ernor Irwin were chartered by the com'
mittee and, well crowded, they steamed
out to the Grant, which lay off Alcatraz.
On the Markham were many -of the of
ficers of Smith's regiment and the ornate
Governor's staff. Mayor Phelan and a
few other celebrities were there. Mrs.
Smith, wife of the general; Mrs. Anna
Smith, his mother; his sister-in-law, Mrs.
FVank Smith, and his son Cyril, all went
out to met him. Seven months ago the
general's wife and son left him in Manila,
but his mother had not laid eyes upon her
son since she held him in her arms as she
said "Good-by and God bless you" the
morning of May 23, 1898. She it was who
was the most eager of the party. Con
strained by age she remained In the pilot
box. Xot so with the wife. On top of
the tug's cabin she secured the best place
for an early discovery and as soon as the
tug was within sight range she spied her
husband on deck. Then there was a
mutual recognition, a waving of hand
kerchiefs and a shouting of welcomes.
When the Markham reached the Grant
the yellow flag Tvas flying on the ship, and
this signal of quarantine prevented an
immediate boarding. ' About an hour later
the quarantine officers gave the ship a
Trips of Tugs to the Grant.
That is why the reception to the gen
eral was without music and noise and en
thusiasm. Preparations had been made
for a. right hearty welcome by a citizens'
committee composed of his quondam com
rades and his other friends. But the
color and life of it was sacrificed on an
altar of respect and reverence- for the de
parted President. As it was. General
Smith felt the sincerity of the greeting-,
expressing his appreciation in smiles and
much handshaking.
The welcome given to General Smith
â¢was rather an informal one, due to the
existence of the national grief. His arrival
on a dull, gray day, with the streets lined
with mourning colors and sorrow in the
air, made his home-coming a quiet affair.
Not until a few hours before landing did
General Smith know of the assassination
or the dea-th of the President- Off Point
Reyes a passing schooner hailed the Grant
and the news was shouted 'across the
waters. "We did not believe it at first,"
said Smith, "but when we caught sight of
the harbor the flags at halfmast told their
tale."
Welcome Is Subdued.
More than three years of the "strenu
ous life" in the islands has left nothing of
an evil mark. General Smith is in line
fettle. His face is just as round, his eye
is as bright and his manner as hearty as
ever it was. He has just 105 days to re
main away from his Judicial labors and
duties and he says he will make the best
of them "in the only land worth living in,
after all."
Like all who have had a. taste of mili
tary adventure and life in the Philip
pines, General Smith is very glad to feel
ihe pavements of his own city under his
feet once again.
STAKDING on the deck of the
transport Grant, smiling and now
and then shaking- hands with him
self because he could not grasp
those of his friends on the tugs
that bobbed up and down on the lee side
of the ship, James F. Smith, general, col
lector of customs and judge, drank in a
bit of the tubdued welcome yesterday
afternoon upon his return to San Fran
cisco.
Three years ago last May "Jim" Smith
marched down Market and Third streets
at the head of the First California Regi
ment, bound for Manila. Since that time
he has become a warrior, a military gov
ernor, a collector of customs and a judge.
This is his first visit to his native land
since that memorable day when all San
Francisco turned out to bid him and the
boys back of him a hearty godspeed. He
was Colonel then. Now "Jim' Smith is
a Justice of the Supreme Court of Manila.
His friends call his plain "Jim," his ac
quaintances "general," the title bestowed
upon him by the Government in recogni
tion of hls-*ervJces.

AUSTIN, Tex., Sept. 18.â Daniel Gug T
genheim; of New York City, chairman of
the -"executive . board-, of .the American
Smelting and Refining Company, passed
through here last night on his way to
Mexico. He said that the object of his
visit to that country at this time is to
urge upon the Government authorities the
advisability of taking off the duty' on
crude oil. He said extensive experiments
had been made with the Beaumont oil In
the refining plant of the smelting trust at
Perth -Amboy, N.. J., and the â¢ results
showed that the new fuel can be used
successfully for smelting purposes.
It is the intention of the company to
substitute oil for coke for smelting pur
poses in all their smelters in Mexico if the
duty. on < the product is removed. The oil
will also be used In the company's smelt
ers in thia country. v Â¦

The progress of the royal pair through
the thronged streets was a prolonged ova
tion. The municipal reception, which
was planned for to-night in honor of the
Duke and Duchess, was abandoned on ac
count of the death of President McKinley,
but the city was brilliantly illuminated
and there was a torchlight procession and
a display of fireworks. As a further
mark of respect to the American people
in their mourning there will be no public
functions to-morrow. The Duke is to re
ceive a degree at McGill University and
he and the Duchess will make a number
of private visits.
At the conclusion of the address 140
veterans of the South African war were
called into the stand and presented with
sen-ice medals. As the Duke handed
each man his medal he briefly thanked
him for his patriotism and valor. The
crowds behind the long lines of troops
cheered wildly as the rewarded soldiers
filed from the stand. At the conclusion
of the ceremony at the station the royal
party were escorted co their carriages
and under guard of a strong military de
tachment were driven to the home of Lord
Strathcona. ' .

CAREER AS SOLDIER.
"American capital Is being : placed In
Manila and in all the islands, but the
English had the lead. Not until very re
cently did the American care to invest
in Philippine properties. Promoters of all
kinds are now on the scene and the Isl
ands really offer good opportunities.
"My home-coming has been sadly damp
ened by the grievous tiews of the Presi
dent's death. It is so new and' fresh to
me that I can't realize it." -
"We have a regular system of -courts
there, the justice and police tribunal and
the Supreme Court. We administer the
civil law, but use our own procedure.
,T^i|s;changQi,was. brought about-since the
American occupation/ and it was one of
absolute, imperative necessity.* Judicial
affairs and all litigation Â¦was so hopelessly
tangled and delays were of such provok
ing insistence that we found it necessary
to make a jurisprudential revolution in re
gard, to procedure.
Is a power back of this Government that
is operating daily for their good. They
now see and appreciate that the law and
order enforced by the United States are
the best they have ever known. Since the
last election the Philippine Islands have
become solidified from the governmental
standpoint.
General Smith has this to say about
Manila: "Conditions are improving rap
idly in the islands and every- day brings a
more settled and advanced state of af
/airs. Tbe. native Is realizing that there
Manila Is Improving.
