NEW YORK. Sept 18.â People who argued
that thr sudden weakness that developed in
the etock market yesterday afternoon after
the early chow of strength was due to the
frost scar* In the corn bolt, found confirmation
for tljelr views In the recover}' In the stock
market to-day simultaneously with the re
action In corn. The loss of all yesterday's rise
in the price of corn showed an eagerness to
realize on Jong lines that took the 'edge' off
the apprehension regarding the reported fur
ther damage to the corn crop. The effect of
thii development was naturally most pro
nounced in the stocks of the great Western
railroads, whose traffic Is large In corn, name
ly, the grangers and Pacifies, Including Atchi
*on and Missouri Pacific. But there was no
reason why the developments In the corn mar
ket should have a sympathetic effect on rall
i-oads In Southern territory, those carrying
anthracite and bituminous coal and the net
work of central traffic and minor Eastern trunk
lines which are usually designated as Junior
Vanderbilts. Prices of all of those stocks were
notably buoyant and the buying of them was
on a scale which could be only for the ac
count of largest local Interests. Buying was
â¢without any tspecific news to explain it. It
was attributed to . the same sources .. as that
Â¦which constituted the determined support of
the market all .through the recent crisis. The
public in Wall street waa Btrong.ln the. con
viction that what are called the Vanderbilt.
Morgan. Gould. Rockefeller and Harriman in
terests were all buyers on a very large scale
of the various stocks In which they are in
terested. Speculators and professional opera
tors exhausted the best powers of conjecture In
striving to discover the motive of this' buy-
Ing. As there was no acute pressure of liqui
dation, the mere purpose to support the mar
ket was not sufficient explanation, as it wab
on last Friday and on Monday. There was
considerable cross trading . early In , the day
which had the appearance of an effort to con
fuse sentiment, brokers usually , acting for

CHICAGO, Sept. 18.â Frost was the dominant
factor again to-day in the corn market and the
opening was excited and higher. The whole
corn belt had been nipped according , to reports
and the feeling -was that yesterday's heavy
buying would be renewed. With this Decem
ber started %@%c higher at 61@60Uc There
was, however, a conspicuous absence of .yes
terday's demand and longs who bought yester
day began to realize profits until December
slumped off to - 5S%c. Frosts were predicted
again for to-night, but that prospect did not
seem to influence the market. Covering 1 by
shorts and general evening up of contracts for
to-morrow's holiday steadied prices at the de
cline and December reacted to 59140. The close,
however, was weak, l%c lower at 58%c.
Wheat was dull and heavy and in the ab
sence of individual Influences followed corn
closely. Outside support was lacking and the
large amount that was carried over last night
was thrown over. December opened %(&>,ic
higher to a shade lower at 72V6@71%c. Liquida
tion set the price down to 70?4c, where the
market became a narrow one. The reaction in
corn brought the price back to 71V4c but De
cember closed weak, %c lower at 71@71Hc.
Oats opened steady. December V4@%c lower
at 37%<337c. Considerable selling by holders
weakened the market along . with corn. The
close was %c lower at 36%c.
Provisions were quiet and steady. They were
higher in sympathy with. the early strength in
grains. January pork closed 7%c up, lard 10c
higher and ribs with a gain of 5c.
Note â There will be no session of the board
to-morrow. â¢ â¢ .

I Feather Report.
CONVICTED OF CUTTING
GOVERNMENT TIMBER
Two Shasta County Settlers Who Â£>
pent of a Recent Speculation
in Shakes.
Thomas McCoy and Collins Ritts wera
convicted by a jury in the United States
District Court yesterday on an indictment
charging them with illegally cutting tim
ber on Government land in Shasta Coun
ty, north of Whitmore. They were or
dered to appear on Saturday for sentence.
Charles H. Ritts, brother of one of the
defendants, was also indicted, but he
kept out of the way of the United Statea
Marshal and has not yet been arrested.
It is alleged that the trio cut a large
quantity of sugar pine off four sections
of Government land, most of which they
made up into shakes.
A civil action is pending in the United
States Circuit Court to recover a large
sum as damages from the persons who
purchased the shakes and lumber from
the defendants.
To Test Cemetery Ordinance.
With the object of testing the constitu
tionality of the ordinance which went
into effect August 1 prohibiting bodies
from being interred â¢- any cemetery
within the city and couaty, J. Hasshageri
of 2200 Grove street swore to a warrant
in' Judge Cabanlsa* court yesterday for the
arrest of George R. Fletcher, superin
tendent of the Odd Fellows' cemetery,
on the charge' of violating the ordinance
by Interring the body of Earl Charles
Prlntero In the cemetery on August 31.
Fletcher was arrested and released on
bail.

May Yet Escape Punishment.
George "Walker, who was convicted of
the embezzlement of JSO J5 from the
American Tract Society about four years
ago and who, by taking advantage cf
legal technicalities has thus far escaped
serving any part of his sentence, has
been granted another hearing by the Su
preme Court. Had the defendant accept
ed his original sentence he would have
been out of Jail months ago.

DECIDUOUS FRUITS,
There was not much going on yesterday. Â¦ A
car of 270 boxes of overripe Bartlett Pears came
in and they were offered down to 50c per box,
but could not be sold. Peaches were in good
supply and dull. Figs' were suddenly plentiful
and the market was much weaker in conse
quence. The market for Wine Grapes ad
vanced, both -here and in the country, and
Table Grapes were steady. Apples were quoted
firm.
Melons are dull, owing to the cold and cloudy
weather. . .
The fruit trade will observe to-day as a
closed day on account of the funeral of the late
President McKinley. â¢ One large house offered
two Market street -peddlers a lot of ripe Bart
lett Pears cheap. TheÂ» replied that they would
buy nothing that they would have to sell to
day, as nothing would Induce them to sell goods
on the day of the President's funeral.
Deciduous and Citrus Fruits.
""New crop Glenoble walnuts, to arrive about
November IS, are reported selling quite treely
at about lC%c. No prices have as yet been
made on California No. 1 softshell, but at the
price talked of, 9c on the coast, California?
could hardly compete in this market. New
filberts for October delivery in New York arc
offered at S%c
Â¦"Evaporated apples are unchanged and easy
on epot. Prime for future delivery are slightly
firmer.
â¢Â¦Currants on spot are very easy and can be
bought practically at buyers' prices. Amalla
grade per first direct steamer, the Manin, are
mostly quoted at 6%c. The Manin. it is un
derstood, brings about 2500 barrels. She left
Denia September 9 and Is due to arrive here
about September 24. New currants for Sep
tember shipment from Greece were quoted
slightly higher then yesterday.
"New crop prunes are easier to get at a 3%@
3%c basis and the trade is taking hold to a
limited extent. Apricots have been a little
easier since early in the week and several cars
of gpot goods have been worked off in small
lots. Peaenes are quiet. Two cars of new
choice Crawfords in bags have arrived and are
ottered nt S%c in bags.
"The spot market continues quiet and prac-
Tically unchanged. Seedless Muscatel raisins
Ere firm at 6%c and about the only article that
Is at ail active in a large way. sales of two
oars being reported. Seeded in cartons, are
held firm at 7'jc for round lots, although stocks
have been increased by arrivals of se\-eral cars
from the coast. The cartons now coming in
bear a red "sticker," but Jt is not the one
bearing the 10c price mark which has been so
objectionable to the trade and which has led
to repacking of coast goods on arrival. Loose
Muscatel raisins are easier, being offered more
lreely at 6%c for 3-crown.

WASHINGTON,
All the exchanges will be closed to-day.
The Makaweli Sugar Company has declared
a dividend of 40c per share, payable on the
25th inst. The directors announce that owing
to the decreased price in raw sugar and in
creased cost of labor there will be no further
dividends until next Fehruary.

VANCOUVER, B. C, Sept. 18.â It was
given out here to-day that a deal is pend
ing between Canadian Pacific officials
and Goodall, Perkins & Co. for the sale
to the latter of the big sidewheeler Yo
semite. The vessel, will be used on the
route between Seattle and Vancouver,
which pays exceedingly well for the two
steamers now operating. The Yosemlte
will handle business by the Pacific Coast
Steamship Company, so that the regular
liners will not have to come to Vancou
ver. The extra passenger rate to San
Francisco, as against the rate from Seat
tle, will be absorbed under the new ar
rangement. -
Buying the Steamship Yosemits.
NEW YORK, Sept. 18.â At a special
meeting of the stockholders of the Ameri
can Sugar Refining Company in Jersey
City to-day the directors were authorized
to cancel the $10,000,000 mortgage bonds
created when the company was organized,
but which were never Issued, and to issue
115,000,000 additional stock. The new stock
will be half common and half preferred,
and present stockholders of each class
will have the privilege of subscribing at
par to the new stock at the rate of one
share of new stock for every five shares
of old stock.
Will Cancel Mortgage Bonds.

As the applications for admission to the
model school classes maintained In con
nection with the State Normal School in
this city are in excess of the accommo
dation from the fifth to the eighth grades,
the board of trustees at their meeting
yesterday decided to charge all grammar
grade pupils who may be admitted after
October 1 an annual tuition fee of $3. The
total attendance is now 123 students. Re
ports show that twenty-five of last year's
graduates have secured positions In eight
een Â»countles of the State. F. A. Hyde
was added to the executive committee.
Make Charge for Tuition.
VANCOUVER, B. C, Sept. 18.â The
story of a probable murder was brought
here by the steamer. Tees. The body of
an unknown man was found burned to a
crisp on the rocky shore of Moresby Island,
400 miles north of Vancouver. Prospec
tors found the charred remains in a
thicket. Large stones had been taken
into this remote spot, the body placed on
the pile and a fire made of dried logs.
The remains were plainly those of a white
man, apparently about 30 years of age.
Found on a Rocky Northern
Shore.
Evidence of Murderer's Awful Crime

KANSAS CITY, Mo., Sept. 18.â The dele
gates representing seven political parties
of reform, who came here to the "Allied
Third party" conference, held their ses
sion to-night, after two days of separate
meetings behind closed doors. All of the
preliminary work, of launching the new
political party, which is to be a union of
all reform parties, was done by the con
ference of committees of the several dele
gations .before the delegates met In joint
session to-night. It was directed that the
party is to be called the Allied party, and
a platform embodying the principles . of
the party was agreed, upon.
Name for the New Party.

Mrs. Josephine Smith, from whom her
husband, Charles H. Smith, obtained a
divorce some time ago, filed notice of a'
motion for a" new trial yesterday. She
also asks to be allowed $50 costs and the
further sum of $250 for counsel fees.
The Smith divorce *case attracted a
great deal of attention during the trial,
owing to the attitude of Mrs. Smith, who
acted as her own lawyer. She conducted
the case in a manner that savored more
of a family quarrel than a legal proceed
ing.
Mrs. Smith Asks for a New Trial.

Carelessness with gasoline in the rooms
occupied by the Golden Gate Renovating
Company caused a lively blaze about 2
o'clock yesterday afternoon in the build
ing at 10, 12 and 14 Montgomery street.
A considerable quantity of clothing was
destroyed. The fire also damaged the
printing establishment of Heyman
Brothers on the floor above. Baldwin &
Hov.ell and G. H. Umbsen & Co., who
occupied offices on the ground floor, were
damaged by water. The building Is
owned by Charles Holljrook of Holbrook,
Merrill & Stetson. The loss was about
$2500, fully covered by insurance.
Fire on Montgomery Street.
PITTSBURG, Sept. 18.â At a meeting to
day of sriking ironworkers from the Star
and MonongahoJa plants, at which 500
were present, it was dec ded by unanimous
vote to repudiate the New York agree
ment and continue the strike independ
ently. Failing to hear from President
Shaffer on the terms of the settlement,
representatives of the two lodges con
ferred with J. W. Phillips, district man
ager for the American, Tinplate Company.
The terms offered by Mr. Phillips, the
men claim, means a big reduction in ton
nage, rates, while the output is greatly
increased. . The men will continue the
strike against this reduction.

Man Who Said He Killed Millionaire
* Rice Seeks to End His

NEW YORK, Sept. 18.-Charles F.
Jones, the valet of William Marsh Rice,
whom Jones said he had killed by admin
istering chloroform, made two attempts
last night to commit suicide. Jones first
tried to cut his throat with the jagged
neck, of a large bottle, which he had
shattered for the purpose. Later he fas
tened his head between the uprights of
his iron bedstead and tried to throw his
body off the bed and thus break his neck.
Both these attempts were frustrated by
the police watching him. Jones had been
feeling badly for several days and wa3
subject to fits of melancholy.

The briquets arc made of a combina

expense is about the same as that of or
dinary forms of fuel, the quantity neces
sary to be consumed to secure the same
amount of heat is about 50 per cent less.
The . plant for the manufacture of the
product Is located in Stockton and has a
capacity of 400, tons daily. ..AIL- the pro
cesses have been patented and the out
look for the venture Is most propitious.
The enterprise Is one which should
prove of great value to California, since
It opens the way to the domestic use of
oil, a goal toward which Inventors have
long striven but which they have not pre
viously attained. The coal utilized con
sists of screenings and slack, which com
bined with oil and placed -under heavy
pressure comes out of the factory in the
form of small oblate spheroids.
The San Francisco and San Joaquin
Coal Company, the pioneers in the man
ufacture of briquets on the Pacific Coast,
placed their new product on the. market
yesterday. Experiments with a view to
perfecting this sort of fuel' for domestic
use have been going on for a period of
more than three years and results have

secretary of the Carpenters' Union wrote:
"No. we don't favor manual training in
schools, as It would give a boy - Ideas, and
we have too many ideas now." The secretary
of the Bricklayers' Union, said: "WÂ« be
llevo that the present manner of producing
mechanics and foreign immigration is fully
equal to the demand." Another &ald:
"Americans are not wanted In the trades.
If educated they would not obey union orders
unless convinced of their wisdom, nor pay as
sessments without an accounting."
By excluding American apprentices 'from
the shops and excluding American boys who
learn trades in the trade schools, and claim
ing the right to inflict the death penalty upon
non-union workmen, foreign influence and
power have effectually deprived American
youth of Its lawful birthright. To the youth
of the land we must look for the preservation
of the spirit of Americanism But deprived
of their birthright we find thousands of Ameri
can boys confined as incorrigibles In the State
reform schools. Idleness has wrought Its evil
work upon them and they fester in the vices
of association, .while the ranks of their perse
cutors are recruited by an immigration that
lakes the places which they should fill.
All this Is done under the pretense that the
high wages of this country ace caused by the
efforts of the union leaders. This I deny. We
had the highest wages in the world when we
were colonies of Great Britain and have con
tinued to advance wages as the productivity
of labor has Increased by the use of labor
aiding machinery. Granting that wages have
been somewhat advanced by union organiza
tion, that advance has been more than dis
counted by the economic losses caused by the
industrial wars declared by the walking dc-le
gates and union leaders. One great establish-,
ment in San Krancisco practically closed *by
the strike of last May was paying $240,000 per
month in wages. The loss to Its workmen
alone now reaches a million of dollars. A
conservative estimate of the loss to Califor
nia by the existing strike is J20.000.000. As
this loss paralyzes trade, bankrupts men and
brings want everywhere, Its final effect i% to
injure labor by producing conditions . that
makes the demand for. It less.
Cannot Tolerate Two Governments.
The American people cannot tolerate j two
governments. The laboring man pays . his
share of taxes to his Government for its pro
tection of his lawful right to labor. But In
addition to this he must pay his initiation
lees and assessments to a union leader or he
is not allowed to earn his bread. It is a Eys
tem oi double taxation, and the tax paid to
the union is 'the price of his license to exer
cise a right which the Government guarantees
to him.
I by no means cry. out against foreign im
migration. If it come here to obey the laws
and be American In act and sentiment. It is
welcome and wholesome. But If It come
here to set up a Government within a Gov
ernment and base that upon the right to as
sassinate Americans who' are lawfully and
loyally following our Industrial occupations
necessary to community life, it is not enly
unwelcome, it is un-American, treasonable and
undeserving of the least toleration. We say
te it, "Take your hand off the American boy.
His person is sacred. He Is the fruit of our
rublic schools. He Is the hope of the repub
lic. He shall learn trades whether you like it
or not, and earn his bread in their practice
whether you license him or not. When you
sentence him to death fpr this, you are at
tempting to assassinate this republic, and' It
will crush you to powder.".
At the conclusion of Coloel Irish's ad
dress five-minute replies were given by
advocates of trades unionism. . M. B.
AVllkins propounded the question, "Why
Is it that if the country is so prosperous
as it is claimed there have been found
enough idle men to take the places of
25,000 strikers?"
Colonel Irish replied that the law of
supply and demand applied here. Strikers
had gone into the country to work and
forced country labor to come to the city.
There were really no idle men.
John Aubrey Jones pleaded for a more
general application of the doctrine of the
brotherhood of man.
"I l-elieve in the brotherhood of man."
said Colonel Irish, "but I- don't believe in
the brotherhood of fifteen or twenty men
that takes a workingman into a blind
alley and beats him into insensibility."

been improved by the labor unions and

tions. .
MEN TO LEAD
PARTY FORCES
The report of the Labor Commissioner of
New York prints the answers of sixty-five
irad> unions showing their policy to be the
-exclusion from the trades of American boys.
The Plasterers' Union In Xew York com
pelled employers to sign an agreement that
no one should be permitted to learn the trade
for seven years. The secretary of the Fin
ish***' Union said: "If a boy become a. full
fledged mechanic in a technical -school he
would not know anything 'about unions." The
Exclusion of American Boys.
The Milwaukee labor riots of 18S6 were led
by two foreigners who had been less than eight
months In this country. The New York Labor
Commissioner reports that labor disturbances
are almost invariably led by foreigners. That
class In the New York Labor Convention of
1S88 demanded repeal of the State laws against
industrial conspiracy and of all laws against
the use of violence toward non-union men. The
Century Magazine, after a comprehensive In
quiry Into the policy of the labor union leaders.
f=aid that "the American boy has no rights
which organized labor Is bound to respect. He
is denied Instruction as an apprentice, and if
he be taught his trade in a trade school he
Is refused admission to nearly all the trade
unions, which are controlled by foreigners.
V.'hlle they reject the American boy they admit
all foreigners with but little regard to their
degree of skill."
These leaders, usually of foreign birth or
sentiments, have found It necessary to have
foreign material in the unions In order to con
trol them. In New Jersey 62 per cent of the
members of the unions are foreigners. In Illi
nois â¬S per cent are foreigners. In New York
a majority are foreigners. Mr. Furuseth, nead
of the San Francisco Water Front Federation,
has recently stated that 60 per cent of the
members of his union are foreigners, not citi
zens of the United States, and only 5 per cent
of them married.
It is pleaded by union leaders, walking dele
gates and business agents, most of them for
eigners or Americans only In the second gen
eration, that labor unions have been the potent
agency In advancing wages. Upon this premise
they base their right to license labor. I deny
this premise. The policy of these leaders is to
make themselves necessary by inciting strikes
and making demands.
Leaders Usually Foreigners.
Trade unionism is antl- American when it as
sumes powers that are denied to Government
and proposes that no man shall be permitted
to labor unless he have its license and permis
sion. The ultimate base of all government is
the power to force submission to its laws. The
assumed right of labor unions to license the
laborer being- the assumption of government
over him naturally rests upon force and is car
ried out by attacks upon the person of the un
licensed laborer who depends upon the laws
of his country to protect his right to work. In
its extreme the penalty put upon him by the
licensers of labor is death. In this code of
unionism capital punishment Is decreed against
any man who works without a union license.
We have all stood for weeks in the presence of
this assertion of power by union leaders, and
the statistics show In that period over 2000
attempts to visit capital punishment upon
American citizens for violation of the union
code. These events make it necessary to in
quire what rights they have under the laws
of their country and what means exist for their
enforcement.
Therefore it Is a mistake to assume that ob
jection to the illegal and Illegitimate use of
the power of such unions is opposition to the
lawful and praiseworthy purposes they may
subserve.
ThÂ« American revolution was a movement to
create one Government In which personal and
property rights would have equal protection of
the law. Prom that time until now It has been
believed that the right of every man to follow
his lawful vocation was primary, that It was
established In our Institutions and that the law
would permit no hand to be raised against it
Our Institutions permit the Iree association of
citizens for lawful purpose common to the as
sociates, and this permission is properly used
by any number of men occupied in the same
calling to form a guild or union for the ad
vancement of their mutual interests. Labor
unions so formed to shorten hours of labor and
increase its wage are lawful and praiseworthy
and there Is no well-founded moral or legal
objection to their existence.

EAST OAKLAND YOUNG LADY WEDS

JUDGE OBJECTS
TO USE OF DYE
Miss Garrie Pauline Hewlitt Becomes Wife
of George Gillott Jamieson at Pretty
Wedding at Home of the Bride's Mother
Scathingly Rebukes the
Plaintiff in a Suit for
Divorce.
Say3 Married Man Has No
Right to Change Color
of His Hair.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
1118 Broadway, Sept. 18.
Because he dyed his mustache Judge
Hall decided this afternoon that Jon>i
Boehrer of Berkeley is /not entitled to a
divorce from his wife, Mary Jane Boehrer.
Judge Hall said he was convinced that
Boehrer had scandalized his family by his
determination to appear young again and
added that he did not think that any man
with a family had a right to change tha
natural color of his hair simply to make
himself, attractive to others.
The testimony of Boehrer*3 wife and two
sons, was to the effect that he had become
infatuated with a young woman who lived
in the neighborhood and the first thing
he did was to dye his mustache, which
was of a sandy color, to a deep black.
He received the woman at his house every
night in spite of the remonstrances of his
sons.
In his own behalf Boehrer denied that
he was or is interested in- the woman,
though he did not deny that he dyed his
mustache for the purpose of creating an
impression.
Judge Hall showed his deep interest in
the dyeing process by asking many ques
tions.
"I am convinced." said Judge Hall In
rendering his decision, "that this family
was greatly scandalized by the fact that
the head of it dyed his mustache and that
he was often seen in the company of the
young woman mentioned during his wife's
absence. It shows that he was carried
away with the idea of making himself
young again, which he had no moral right
to do. His sons testified that they had to
listen to ridicule on their father's account.
He is responsible for his own troubles."
Judge Hall denied the application of the
husband for divorce and ordered that he
pay his wife 540 monthly for the support
of their children.

Senator Kills a Phyiscian.
BATON ROUGE, La., Sept. 18.-Dr.
John W. McKown of Clinton, Iowa, wa:?
shot and killed to-day by State Senator
R. E. Thompson of East Feliciana parish.
Some time ago Thompson was arrested a:
the instance of McKown for the alleged
stealing 1 of the fencing b( ween Thomp
son's place and the plantation of Mrs.
Pipes, the sister^ of McKown, and this
caused a bitter enmity. The two men
met to-day in the public road. Thompson
asserts that McKown assaulted him and
that he shot McKown in self-defense.
Thompson surrendered.

Mrs. Mary Jobson,* otherwise known as
Dolly Maxwell, a waif on the Barbary
Coast, died yesterday morning In . the
Adelphia House. 913 Kearny street. She
had been drinking with Fired Anderson in
the Vandalla saloon and was taken sick
â¢while there. Anderson took her to the
Adelphia House, where she told him that
she had taken morphine. A physician Â¦was
sent for, but too long a time had elapsed
since she took the poison and he was
unable to save her.
Suicide of Mary Jobson.
OnÂ© of the prettiest home weddings of
the year occurred las* Tuesday eVenlng ,
when H. Bostwick, who holds a respon
sible position with the Western Union
Telegraph Company, was married to Miss
Lillian Parsons, the daughter of Mr. and
Mrs. E. Parsons, at the home of tha
bride's parents, 4331 Twenty-third street.
The wedding ceremony was performed by
Rev. John A. Every. The parlors, were
beautifully decorated for the occasion
with palms and choice cut hothouse
flowers. Only the immediate relatives
were present. The happy couple left for
a tour of Southern California and expect
to be gone several weeks. On the re
turn of Mr. and Mrs. Bostwick they will
reside in this city.
Parsons at Her Parents'
Home.

Mrs. Fuller also charges that her hus
band has conveyed to Bertha A. Smith
four holdings on First avenue, a piece of
land on Tenth avenue and the residence
at 323 Seventh avenue. She asks that %
restraining order be issued to- prevent her
husband disposing ot any property pend
ing the suit. The Smith woman Is named
as co-defendant in the suit.
Mrs. Fuller claims that her husband's
holdings are valued at $150,000, and that it
Is all community property. She says ha
Is trying to evade the community prop
erty law by encumbering and disposing
of the property.
A suit for maintenance was filed in tha
County Clerk's office yesterday by Emily
T. Fuller, wife of George H. Fuller, the
well known capitalist. In the complaint
Mrs. Fuller alleges that, though her hus
band enjoys an Income of $25,000 a year,
he allows her but J5 a week on which to
keep house. She asks the court to grant
her a monthly allowance of 1350, which
sum. she claims, is necessary to keep her
in accordance with her station In life. The
complaint further alleges that Fuller
compels his wife to go to his office every
Saturday to collect the miserable pittance
he allows her, and that he has warned her
repeatedly that in the event of her ex
penses exceeding $5 per week he would
publish in the papers that he would not
be responsible for the debts contracted by
her.
monious Conduct by His Wife,
Emily T. Fuller.
George H. Fuller Accused of Parsi-

The McKenzie Musical Society has post
poned its concert, programmed for this
evening at Odd Fellows' Hall, until next
Thursday night, September 26.
The Chutes and Zoo postpones its usual
amateur night until to-morrow In respect
to the day of mourning.
At Fischer's Concert-house this week's
programme Includes "The Beautiful Gala
tea," .Suppe's operetta; Mae Tunison. so
prano; the Gagnoux, Jugglers; the Bern
axds. sketch artists; Dilges, the celebrated
aerial perforrner, and many other cele
brated vaudevillians. Fischer's will be
closed to-night.
The Orpheum's programme for this
week is one of .the best ever presented at
this popular place of amusement. Dor
othy Morton, justly styled the "queen of
comic opera," is singing at her best. "Tha
Bridegroom's Beverie," presented by the
Miases Delmore and Walter Caryll. is onÂ©
of the prettiest performances ever given
in San F"ranicsco, and Ernest Hogan in
his coon songs is simply Irresistible. Mr.
and Mrs. Karsy extract wonderful music
from their "giant myriophone" and
George W. Leslie and his company of
farceurs score a tremendous hit In
"Chums." Libby, Trayer and Gilbert are
applauded to the echo In their musical
sketch, "The Debutante." and Stella Lee,
the clever dancer, and Marsh and Sartella.
novelty performers, complete a good pro
gramme. The biograph pictures, by the
way, are unusually Interesting. The Or
pheum will close to-night.
This is the last week of Belasco &
Thall's big production of "A Voice From
the Wilderness" at the Central Theater
and no one should miss it if only for its
spectacular beauties. "Little Lord Faun
tleroy" Is the succeeding play to be put
on on Monday evening next. To-day and
this evening the theater will be closed.
The doors of the Columbia Theater -will
be closed throughout to-day as well as to
night. Â« Friday night, Saturday matinee
and Saturday and Sunday nights Thomas
Q. Seabrooke will continue to present
Sydney Rosenfeld's comedy, "A Modern
Crusoe."* It will continue throughout next
week. "Florodora," of which so much
has been heard during the last year, la
announced as the succeeding attraction at
the Columbia Theater. Many of the prin
cipals who have appeared in the New
York production of the. great musical
success are coming here with the com
pany.
There will be closed doors at the Al
cazar Theater to-night and all of to-day.
The very good little production of "The
Taming of the Shrew," that has been so
popular with the patrons of the Alcazar,
will hold the boards for the remainder of
the week, and next week, that marks the
farewell week of Miss Florence Roberts'
engagement at the Alcazar, will bo given
over to an elaborate revival of "Sapho,"
in which the favorite actress fines per
haps her best opportunity. Â¦ .
S The Tivoll box office will be closed to
day and the theater this evening on ac
count of the funeral of the President.
Everything is sold out for "Carmen"
and "Faust" has run the Bizet opera a
close race in public favor. "Carmen"
will be given again next week, alternat
ing with the new. production of "Na
bucco." which takes place on Monday
evening, September 23. Salassa has the
name part in the opera and is said to be
at his best. Dado. Barbareschl and Pol
lettini are the other principals.
All theaters and places of amusement in
San Francisco will be closed to-day and
this evening in respect to the funeral of
the President. At the Grand Opera
house "Richelieu," In which Mr. Ha.worth
Is doing such good work, will hold the
boards for the remainder of the week.
Mr. Haworth is very well supported by
Miss Florence Stone as Julie de Mortemar
and Herschel Mayal is also winning favor
for himself.

Agents and Tlieir Territories.
The division of colonist rates between
the . transcontinental and Eastern lines
has been satisfactorily settled and the
same rates will prevail this coming sea
son. "The exact date of these rates be
coming effective has not yet been settled
upon, but it i will be some time in the late
fall or early spring. Last season over
20,000 people were brought into the State
on these colonist tickets. Â¦Â¦Â¦ It is not known
how many of them settled here, but, as
the tickets were all one way fares, it is a
fair supposition that a large proportion
remained here. The rates in auestion are
$30 frcm Chicago, $25 from Omaha and
Kansas City and $27 50 from St. Ixmis,
Memphis and New Orleans.
Colonist Kate Advantages.
All of those attending these conventions
are representative, substantial citizens
and will do much toward the prosperity
of the places they visit. Some idea may
be had of .the advantages \ that accrue
from these, immense excursions when one
is told that at the recent meeting of the
Mystic Shriners in- Toronto . the . local
banks did a business of $1,500,000 in excess
of their usual amount. The recent meet
ing of, the Knights Templar in Louisville
brought over I&00.000 to that city.
The Kpworth Leaguers who have ; re
turned to their homes are loud in their
praises of California, its climate, peopfe
and. scenery, and they are doing much to
advertise the -Pacific. Coast '.as , an ideal
place of permanent residence..- . â¢ â¢ - â¢

mentioned the fact that the Southern-Pa

palians, who are about due here ana who
expect -to come about 7000 strong.,; Tne
Women's Clubs Convention has.beeji se

will bring about 50W visitors to the State.
The Mystic Shriners meet here next year
and they expect a representation of over
8000. The Knights Templar will hold their
next triennial conclave here. Thia will not
take place,, however, until some, time in

In speaking of traffic matters in general
Manager McCormick said that California
was fast becoming as popular a summer
resort as it is a winter, one. People are
beginning to appreciate the evenness of
the climate of this State and to awaken
to the fact that it offers as comfortable
a refuge from the fierce heat of an East
ern summer as it does from the pneumo
nia-breeding winds of an Eastern winter.
He says that he se'es no reason why sum
mer travel in the near future should not
fully equal the winter travel of the past.

Cormick of the Southern Pacific Company
has returned to the city after an absence
of about two months. When questioned
yesterday concerning the changes in the"
presidency of the company -and the prob
able retirement of some of the lesser ex
ecutive officers McCormick said he was
in entire ignorance of the intentions of his
superiors j in these ' matters and | that all
he knew of the rumored changes was
what he had read in the papers. â¢

COLONIST RATES
TO BE RESTORED
SHARP LETTER
TO GOVERNOR
* Board of Trade Accuses
Him of Dodging the
â¢ Issue.
Also Finds Inconsistency in
Position of the Ex
â¢ ecutive.
General Chipman, as president of the
State Board of Trade, has addressed a
stinging letter to Governor Gage. Therein
the Governor is accused of dodging the
issue that the State Board raised when it
expressed the opinion that protection
ought to be given to those who stood
ready to handle grain at Port Costa. The
general uses sarcasm in a passage as fol
lows:
Your elaborate exposition of the duties of
the executive and the limitations placed upon
him by the constitution and laws of the State
is wholly gratuitous, since nothing in the
memorial called for such treatment.
There is a sting: in the concluding para
graph, which reads as follows:
It is to be regretted that this phase of the
matter, wholly of your own creation, took
f>uch possession of your mind that you en
tirely overlooked the prayer of the memorial.
As the letter from Governor Gage \ was
addressed to General Chipman and Secre
tary Dennison of the State Board of
Trade, General Chipman has answered for
the board. The text of his communica
tion is as follows:
Text of Stinging Letter.
SAN FRANCISCO. SÂ£pt. 18, 1D01.
Hon. Henry T. Gage, Governor of Califor
nia. Sacramento, Cal. â Sir: Your reply to the
memorial of the California State Board of
Trade is Just received. You have so entire
ly misconceived and misstated the object of
the memorial that in justice to the board I
feel impelled to notice your communication.
An examination of . the memorial will dis
close no suggestion whatever that you call
out the militia, or that you resort to \ any
particular remedy in aid of the fanners who
are unable to move their grain because of
the pending strike. Tour elaborate exposition
of the duties of the executive and the limita
tions placed upon him by the constitution and
laws of the State is wholly gratuitous, since
nothing in the memorial called for such treat
ment. The memorial had no other object than
to call attention to the conditions which were
believed to exist and were resulting in great
loss and damage to the farming industry of
the State. A belief was expressed that if pro
tection could be given to those who stood
ready to handle the grain of the State at
Port Costa all turther embargo on grain move
ments would at once cease, and within a rea
sonable time and before the rainy season had
commenced the grain of the State could be put
under shelter in the warehouses. It was an
unwarranted assumption on your part that
you were expected forthwith to call cut the
militia; nothing was further from the thought
of the board than that. The conditions to
which reference was made were such as
seemed to the board to call for investigation
at least, and it was but reasonable to ask
a.t your hands the application of such remedy
as v.-ould give "all who wished to work the
protection of our laws, if they were not now
receiving such protection. Hence the prayer
cf the memorial:
Admission by GoVemor.
"We therelore respectfully request at your
hands such investigation of the facts as may
disclose to the executi\-e mind the necewary
remedy and the earliest practical .application
of such remedies."
If I do not misinterpret your reply, your po
sition is that you are powerless to interfere
In any way, except upon the request of the
Sheriff of Contra Costa County, and upon
euch showing by him as brings the case with-
Sn your conception of your power under the
constitution and laws. You quote the Sheriff
as saying: "If the Board of Supervisors of
Contra Costa County will defray the expenses
of pÂ° ss e comitatus I believe the trouble would
Boon be ended." Here is certainly an admis
sion that there is trouble requiring official
intervention of some character, and if given,
euch trouble "would socn be ended."
I do not vouch- for its truth, but it is etated
that a cbmmittee * of farmers of the State
composed of William Beckmann. J. Craig and
J. H. Rice, all well known citizens of this
.State, have recently called upon the Sheriff
of Contra Costa County for protection to labor
in handling the grain at Port Ccsta; that the
Sheriff made answer, as he did to you, that
he could take care of the situation If the
Supervisors would pay the expense, which he
has no assurance they will do. It appears,
therefore, that the Sheriff of Contra Costa
County is as helpless as you confess yourself
to be. Under the circumstances the farmers'
committee, it is stated, have determined to
recommend to the fanners that they come to
Port Costa with their men and in such force
as to be able to protect themselves. It it. to
be hoped that if the officers of the law can
find no justification for securing the safety
of men who desire to work they will at least
find no warrant for Interfering to prevent
those ircm working who desire to work and
stand ready to protect themselves.
â¢The board expressed no opinion, and has
none, as to who are responsible for the pres
ent situation. It respectfully Invited your at
tention to the condition and not its origin.
The board did not request you to call out the
militia, or to substitute military for civil au
thority. The discussion of these questions by
you was therefore not called for by the me
morial. It Is to be regretted that this phase
of the matter, wholly of your own creation,
look such possession of your mind that you
entirely overlooked the prayer of the memorial.
Blncerely yours. N. P. CHIPMAN,
President California State Board of Trade.

. Rabbi Jacob Voorsanger delivered last
evening at the Mark Hopkins Institute of
Art his first of the series of free lectures
now being given by the University of Cal
ifornia. The lecture was in the nature of
a general introduction to those that are
to follow on "The Literary History of the
Old Testament." The reverend gentleman
cut away from the usual orthodox man
ner of handling the subject and gave an
interesting narration. of the law, the his
tory and tradition and the poetry of the
Bible. The lecturer said that probably
the greatest difficulty we have in getting
a clear understanding of the Bible is
caused by the fact that it is an Oriental
work, written in an Oriental language
and atmosphere, and comes to us, an Oc
cidental/people, translated into an Occi
dental tongue, and that a literal transla
tion is therefore next to the impossible.
The lecture was attended by a cultured
and attentive audience. As factors in
public education too much cannot be said
about this work of the university, arid the
large attendance and .interest displayed
promise well for the rest of the series.
Dr. Voorsanger's Lecture.

United States District Judge de Haven
dismissed the proceedings in habeas
corpus Tuesday instituted on behalf of
John F. Corriea, convicted recently in the
Police Court of having had more than
fifty wild doves in his possession on one
calendar day. Corriea contended in his
petition that that portion of the State
game law was in violation of the consti
tution of the United States.
Judge de Haven said that In his opinion
the State law did not violate the Federal
constitution. He was of the opinion, how
ever, that the law was passed in the Inter
est of sportsmen's clubs and that the
Legislature should not have passed it and
: the- Governor should not have signed it,
but" as it was. not in conflict ,vjith the Fed
eral constitution he had no jurisdiction in
the matter. ' Corriea was ordered re
manded into custody.
